BELIEVES PEARY WILL
REACH THE NORTH POLE
NEW YORK, Sept. 14.â Herbert 1,.
Bridgeman, who commanded the Peary
Arctic expedition of ls'.t'j, arrived in
Brooklyn to-day. The Diana, the ship
which conveyed the members of the ex
pedition into the northern seas, reached
Sydney. C. 8.. Tuesday. The members of
the Princeton scientific party remained
in Sydney to superintend the shipping of
their collections made during the two
months' cruise.
Of Peary's work Mr. Bridgeman said:
"Peary's results in the first year of the
four allotted to his greatest Arctic work
will fully reward expectation and justify
confidence. What he has already achieved
removes his undertaking from the realm
of doubt to reasonably .certain success."
Mr Bridgeman believes that Peary will
accomplish his purpose and reach the pole
before his four years are up.

STAGE HELD
OP BY A LONE
HIGHWAYMAN
Daring Robbery of the Lake
port Stage at Desperate
Bend.
A SUSPECT ARRESTED
Effort Will Be Made to Fasten the
Guilt on Him With
Bloodhounds.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NAPA, Sept. 14.â The Napa, Calistoga
and Lakeport stage was held up at 11:45
this morning by a lone highwayman at
Desperate Bend, about one mile south
of the tollhouse. The highwayman was
about five feet and eight inches high,
had light hair and a light complexion.
He had on a black hat, pulled down
over his e*yes, and blue overalls, and
carried a long old-fashioned double
barreled shotgun.
Pointing his gun at the driver's head
the highwayman ordered him to throw
out the express box, which he did. The
passengers were next commanded to
put their money and valuables on the
ground. They were then permitted to
get back into the stage and the driver
was told to go ahead. The robber
gathered up all the valuables, sprang
over a fence and disappeared in the
brush. Soon afterward the passengers
heard three explosions, which were
supposed to be the blowing open of the
express box.
Rev. C. F. Coy, pastor of the Metho
dist Church at Middletown. was a pas
senger returning from the conference
at Pacific Grove and the highwayman
got $5 from him. Coy said, "'I am a
poor preacher and that is all I have."
The robber then handed him back $1.
Newton Stiff, an old resident of Mid
dletown, another passenger, had con
siderable money in his purse, which he
slipped into his boot, giving the rob
ber only his loose change. William
Abbey, a passenger who was on his
way to Adams Springs for his health,
was handed back 50 cents. About $75
was secured from the passengers. The
express box contained very little treas
ure, though the day before it contained
a large amount.
CALISTOGA, Sept. 14.â Sheriff Dun
lap and Constable Allen of Napa and
Constable Spurr and City Marshal
Johnson of St. Helena arrived in town
at 5 o'clock. C. E. Buller joined the
officers here with his bloodhounds, for
merly owned by ex-Sheriff J. R. John
son f>f Mendocino County, and the offi
cera are ra hoi pursuit of the fugitive.
It is believed by some that the rob
ber is George Coburn, the Lake County
outlaw. If so there will be a desperate
battle before he is taken into custody,
if at all. Coburn is so thoroughly ac
quainted with the country that his cap
ture would be almost out of the ques
tion. Others are of the opinion that it
is not Coburn, but a local man. The
description given by some of the pas
sengers is that of a rather tall, slim,
sandy-complexioned fellow.
At 7 o'clock Constable Grauss and
Marshal Nash returned with Joe Weir,
a well-known character, whom they
have arrested as the robber. Quigley,
the driver, made a statement at the
tollhouse that he was satisfied that it
was Weir who held him up. With this
clew to work on the officers soon found
evidence against the accused. Weir
lives about a mile from the scene of the
hold-up, all alone. The bloodhounds
have been started on the track and if
they go to Weir's house no further
search â¢will be made.
MONETARY CRISIS
OCCURS IN SAN DOMINGO
Paper Money Refused and the Poor
People Thus Rendered Unable
to Buy rood.
Special Cable to The Call and Aw York Her
ald. Copyrighted, 1899, by James Gordon
Bennett.
SAN DOMIXGO. Sept. 14.â A crisis was
reached In the monetary situation to-day.
Merchants refused to take paper money
and the people were unable to purchase
food. This resulted in serious disturb
ances in the market place this morning
and great disorder prevailed.
A Cabinet meeting was hurriedly called
and a proclamation practically demon
etizing paper money, except Tn part pay
ment of customs duties, was issued. The
proclamation was hissed and is certain
to cause great disssatisfaction and suf
fering.
General Caceres has resigned as Minis
ter of War and Aristides Patins, former
Minister, succeeds him. The Government
promises relief when Congress meets, but
the people demand immediate action.

BAKERSFIELD, Sept. 14.â Three
women and one man lost thefr
lives in an awful wreck last even-
Ing at Famosa station. The last
victim. Engineer Ed Wright of
north-bound passenger, train No. 8,
succumbed at 8:30 o'clock this morning.
The names of the dead women are: Mrs.
Maggie Majors, Santa Monica; Mrs.
Angie Larue, Piano, and Mrs. Jessie
Ross, Porterville. The latter was the
mother of Mrs. Majors. Mrs. Majors
was instantly killed and her crushed
body was found over one hundred feet
from the wrecked caboose. Mrs. Larue
lived an hour and Mrs. Ross three
hours. â¢ The bodies of both were badly
crushed.
The three women and Trackwalker
Bockstrandt were the only persons on
the caboose when it was struck. Brock
strandt was on the front platform and
was knocked several feet. He sustained
a broken leg and a .serious .cut on the
I face and head and a large splinter was
driven through his right arm. He is in
a serious condition and has been sent
!to the Oakland hospital. After the en
! gine had demolished the caboose and
; two boxcars of the Porterville train It
turned over on its right side. The im
pact of the collision threw the coal for
ward in the cab, covering , Engineer
Wright so that he could not extricate
; himself and there he was held and
! scalded by steam which escaped from
| where the whistle had been broken off.
i Fireman Keller, strange to relate, es
caped with only a few bruises. He can
not tell how it happened. He only
1 knows that he found himself on, Â£ the
i ground ; beside .tho overturned 'engine. "
Before he died, notwithstanding his
great agony, brave Engineer Wright
stated that he did all he could. When
he saw he must strike the caboose he
i turned on the full force of air and re
versed the engine, which literally lifted
the tram from the track; making such
a commotion that people half a- mile
away thought it was an earthquake.
He did not see the caboose until he
was within two hundred feet of it. Its
rear lights were dark.
When the air was turned on the pas
sengers were pitched pellmell from
their seats, many of them being more
or less bruised and cut. One lady's
arm was broken. The baggage car
, withstood the shock, but the coach next
to it, being old and having spliced sills,
could not resist the force of the col
j lision and about twelve feet of the for
ward end crumpled up against the rear
of the baggage car. Here several wo
men were found securely fastened in
the wreck, fortunately uninjured, save
for a few slight scratches.
The two boxcars and caboose of the
Porterville train were smashed to kind
ling wood and the engine thrown from
the track. The Porterville train was
in the act of coupling on to the caboose
when the passenger struck it. Brake-
I man Lowe, who had the pin, saw what
was coming and quickly got out of tho
way.
Those who reached the scene of the
collision soon after it happened were
dumfounded at the horrible sight that
met their gaze, but soon fell to work
to help the injured and dying. In a
little while the dead and dying were
borne to the depot, about two hundred
yards away and placed on improvised

surgeons arrived from Bakersfleld they
were at once given attention.
Coroner Buckreus and Deputy Dis
trict Attorney Dorsey were also taken
to the scene on a special train and took
observations. A jury was impaneled
and the dead bodies viewed. Both the
train crews and other witnesses to the
affair were summoned to appear here
at 10 o'clock Sunday and give testi
mony at the Investigation.
After this the bodies of the three
women were taken to Porterville on a
special train and the body of Engineer
Wright was taken to Fresno. Mrs.
Wright arrived on a special train four
hours before her husband died. The
meeting was touching.
Some time after the wreck occurred
the train dispatchers at Kern denied
repeatedly that any one was hurt. The
reason they now offer is that no one
was present to take the responsibility
of giving out such information. They
were willing to tell of the wreck, but
were afraid to tell that any one was
hurt. They claim it is against the rules
to talk, notwithstanding the fact that
the public was interested.
The names of the Porterville train
crew are: Conductor, W. A. Kessler;
engineer, Klwood; fireman, A. S. Be
ford; brakemen, Walter Lowe, C. H.
Buck and Frank Thurman. These are
the men on whom the blame of the
wreck will fall.
From information elicited since the
collision it is learned that No. 8, the
passenger train, had been given orders
here to wait until 10 o'rlock at Famosa
for a south-boynd freight. It was seen
before No. 8 was ready to go out that
the freight could not make Famosa by
that time and the order was annulled
to the freight and to the passenger
train. The operator at Famosa told the
trainmen of the Porterville train of the
order which would have protected them
until 10 o'clock but for the annulment,
of which they did not learn. The freight
stopped at Hunt, the next station north,
and the passenger, which left Bakers
field fifteen minutes late, was only
eleven minutes late at Famosa, and it
was the remaining four minutes upon
which the Porterville train crew count
ed to enable them to get off the main
line. The blame all rests with the crew
of the Porterv .le train, who well knew
they took desperate chances while oc
cupying the main line on the passenger
train's time.
It is further stated that the Porter
ville train has in the past been known
to carelessly loiter on the main line too
close on the time of through trains. All
who have visited the scene of the wreck
declare that somebody Is criminally
responsible for its occurrence and -the
cruel loss of life.
The blame for the terrible accident at
Famosa is attributed by the Southern
Pacific officials to the crew of the
freight train. They say that they can
see no reason for the accident except in
the gross carelessness of those having
charge of the freight train and in their
utter disregard of every rule of rail
roading in switching on the main track
without taking the proper precautions
to protect their train from even the
slightest possibility of a collision. Their
conduct appears to be all the more
reprehensible from the fact that the
passenger train was nine minutes be
hind time and those directing the
freight could not but have known that
it was expected to arrive at any mo
ment.
Moreover, the Southern Pacific .offi
cials say, that even if the train was on
the main track against orders they can
not understand how the accident could
have occurred, as the country there is
perfectly level and has nothing along
the line of track which should pre
vent an obstruction as large as a
freight train being seen for many miles
in the daytime, and on a clear night it
f'ould easily be perceived in plenty of
time to prevent any such mishap as
the one which has just caused such an
appalling loss of life.

STRANGE UPHEAVAL OF
LAKE CHELAN WATERS
Theory Advanced That a Volcano
Caused the Rushing Forth of a
Great Tidal Wave.
SPOKANE, Wash.. Sept. 14. -Word has
reached here of a recent peculiar occur
rence at Lake Chelan, the largest lake In
Washington, situated about 2"0 miles
west of here, in the heart of a mountain
range. Last Sunday persons living along
the lake shore saw an^upheaval of the
waters near the lake center. Huge
waves, some of them ten feet high,
started from this point and rolled toward
the shore. At one point, where a small
! issenger steamer was moored, the hoat
â¢was torn loose by the waves and carried
ashore, when the waves receded it was
Carried back, overturned and swamped.
No person was aboard at the time.
The disturbance continued several
hours. A thunder and lightning storm
â was taking place at the time, hut there
Ittle wind blowing. The only theory
given is that the upheaval was of vol
canic origin. In tin- same vicinity at about
une time, a large creek, one of the
feeders of the lake, went dry for three
Indicating that the disturbance ex
tended into the mount-.irs.
Indians living in the \ieinity of the lake
t ,iv that similar occurrences have taken
ii the memory of the tribe. For
reason few of the red men can he
induced to venture out on the lake, even
in the nr.o&t pleasant weather.

THE BRITISH COMMERCIAL
EXPEDITION IS SAFE
After Having Failed to Explore Siberian Streams, Owing to
the Loss of the Ship by Ice, the Explorers Finally
Reach Norway,
BT. PETERSBURG, Sept. 14.â A dispatch from Hammerfest, the
northernmost town of Norway, announces the arrival there of the British
commercial expedition, which vainly attempted to explore the mouth of
the river Obi and other Siberian streams.
The expedition was turned back by the ice, after losing its ship, the
Arthur, which was crushed by the floes. Fortunately there was no loss
of life.

WELL RECEIVED
Given Enthusiastic Wel
come Home.
Pppcia! Piarntch to The Call.
DENVER. Sept. 14.â The Colorado vol
unteers were welcomed? home on their re
turn to the State's capital to-day with a
demonstration that atoned for the lack of
enthusiasm manifested when the regi
ment departed from this city on May 17,
18Â»S. on its long journey to the Philippines.
Bells, whistles, guns, cannon and horns
began sounding a noisy welcome when the.
soldiers' free train reached the Union
depot at 10 o'clock.
After receiving the greetings of rela
tives the volunteers fell in line and were
escorted by a procession to the Capitol
through streets lined with masses of peo
ple waving rlags and shouting cheers. All
the heroes of the Philippine campaign
were crowned with wreaths of mountain
holly and their pathway was strewn with
flovers by hundreds of girls. Public and
private buildings were handsomely deco
rated. On the Capitol grands were as
sembled thousands of school children,
who sang "America" in chorus. An ad
dress of welcome was delivered by Gov
ernor Thomas, who presented a new flag
to the regiment. Colonel McCoy made
an appropriate reply. Addresses of wel
come on the part of the nation were de
livered by United States Senators Wol
cott and Teller, to which response was
made by Brigdaier General Irving Hale. A
banquet was served for the volunteers
ami during the remainder of the day they
enjoyed the freedom of the town.

DISASTROUS FLOODS IN
GERMANY AND AUSTRIA
Bridge Undermined and Train Falls
Into River â Panic Occurs in
Synagogue.
BERLIN, Sept. 14.â The floods are
causing widespread damage in Southern
Germany and Austria. The northerly part
of Zwickau is inundated. Owing to the
undermining of the railway bridge over
the Isen between Muelldorf and Rohrsdorf
a train fell into the river and five per
sons were killed. All the tributaries of
the Danube are flooded. A bridge over
the Swarza at Pajwrbach collapsed and
ten persons were carried away.
Atlvices from Kaliscb, in Russian Po
land, say that thirty-two persons were
crushed to death in a panic In a syna
gogue there, caused by the upsetting of
a lamp. The victims were all women and
children. Many others were injured.
SALZBURG, Austria, Sept. 14.â Part of
this city and places in the vicinity have
been flooded. Troops have beefl called
out to tender assistance. The water Is
now subsiding slowly.
MUNICH, Sept. 14.â The new Prince Re
gent bridge here collapsed this afternoon
in consequence of a rise of the River Isar,
which is still at flood level. There were
do casualties.

MINE AWARDED
TO JOSEPH KAHNY
Decision on a Puzzling
Land Law.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING. Sept 14.â Word has just
been received by Attorney F. W. Smith
that the Secretary of the Interior has de
cided that the three mining claims situ
ated between Taylor postoffice and the
town of Keswick belong to Joseph Kahny
of this city.
The decision settles an important point
in mining law and quiets title on property
over which murder has been committed
since the suit began. About a year ago
Joseph Kahny applied' to the United
United States Land Office for a patent on
two quartz and one placer claim. nv niie
the application was going through the
usual channel incident to its granting tne
claims were' jumped by John Pendleton
and J." H. O'Hair of Keswick. They con

PRAISE FOR SECRETARY
OF WAR ELIHU ROOT
Governor Steunenberg of Idaho Ex-
presses His Satisfaction at the
Reception of Volunteers.
WASHINGTON. Sept. 14.-The War De
partment has given out the following:
SAN FRANCISCO, Cal., Sept. 5, 1899.â
Hon. Elihu Root, Secretary of War,
Washingtonâ Dear Sir: In company with
Senator George L. Shoup. the Adjutant
General of Idaho, and other citizens of
my State, I arrived in San Francisco the
last week in August for the purpose of
welcoming the Idaho volunteers return
ing from the Philippine Islands.
In my capacity as an official of the
State of Idaho, it gives me much pleas
ure to inform you that the arrangements
made by the War Department officials of
the Department of California for the re
ception, care and maintenance" of these
troops, their' accommodations in camp
and the courtesies extended to our people
have been ample and gratifying.
Knowing the groat interest that the
War Department has taken in all matters
pertaining to the volunteer army, I have
much pleasure in adding my testimony to
that of other Governors of the various
States whose troops have been received
here and are now being mustered out.
Your obedient servant.
FRANK STEUNENBERG,
Governor of Idaho.
INDIANS ENLISTED TO
FIGHT THE FILIPINOS
Chippewas, Nez Perces and Others in
a Company Commanded by
Captain Tillman.
WASHINGTON. Sept. 14.â James H.
Tillman, a son of ex-Representative fill
man of South Carolina, who served as
colonel of the Second South Carolina Vol
unteers in the Spanish war, has been
promised by the President a captaincy in
one of the mw volunteer regiments.
Colonel Tillman came to Washington hop
ing to raise an independent company of
scouts. The President did not approve of
forming any independent commands at
this time, but said there was no objection
\o enlisting a certain proportion of In
dians in a company which he authorized
Captain Tillman to organize.
Lieutenant A. A. Girard, formerly of the
Rough Riders, has been named as First
Lieutenant of Captain Tillman's company
One platoon of the company will be com
posed of educated Indians â leading men
of the Chippewas. Nez Perces and other
iribes.
Poisoned by Alcohol.
VALLEJO, Sept. 14.â Two marines,
Michael Owens and Richard Conroy of
the United States ship Philadelphia, ob
taining some wood alcohol aboard ship,
proceeded to imbibe freely. As a result
Owens died yesterday morning and Con
roy last night. Conroy has a brother in
bbslnesa in San Francisco.
Good Grain Crop.
WOODLAND. Sept. 14.â A Oransmore
dispatch to the Democrat says: "The
transportation companies on the river are
finding it a difficult matter to move the
immense crop of grain. Boats can carry
only half a load on account of the low
stage of water. The latest acquisition to
the grain fleet is a Stockton harge with a
capacity of 8000 sacks."

MRS. MANUEL FATALLY
BURNED AT FRESNO
She Attempted to Blcrw Out a Lamp,
When It Exploded, Setting
Fire to Her Clothing.
FRESNO. Sept. 14.â Mrs. George Man
uel, the wife of a civil engineer, died at
a:: early hour this morning' from burns.
She conducted a lodging-house at 2227
Merced street and on retiring- last night
attempted to blow out a coaloil lamp. The
lamp exploded setting fire to her clothing.
She was very badly burned. The fire
itself was insignificant and was speedily
extinguished

MILITARY AND CIVIL
OFFICIALS AT THE FAIR
Day of Sightseeing and Handshaking Termi
nates With a Banquet in the Assembly
Chamber of the Capitol Building,
Scenes and Incidents at the State Fair.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. , Such a
scene as was witnessed, in this city
to-night has never been equaled in
the history of the great State of
California, for the extraordinary
enthusiasm that prevails in connection
with the State Fair is the attending cir
cumstances of Governor's day and Sacra
mento day combined.
The Governor of California was not only
the guest of the directors of the State
Agricultural Society, but he was the
guest also of the citizens of this city rep
resented in their citizens' State Fair Club,
to whom is due nearly all the credit for
the genuine success of the forty-sixth an
nual State Fair. To make the occasion
a momentous Â«ne high United States mil
itary and naval officers were invited.
They came in large numbers and were
graciously received by the people of the
community and In a manner such as
others have seldom been received before.
As a result of the efforts put forth by
those connected directly and indirectly
with the management of the fair to make
it the success it surely is the pavilion
was packed to-night with humanity.
Old people who have resided in this city
from early childhood say that they have
never before seen such a large number
of people in the pavilion at any one time.
* The information that Major General
Shatter of the United States.army and
Rear Admiral Kautz of the United States
navy and their attending officers and
prominent National Guard officers would
attend the fair had much to do with the
general outpouring of the populace. Their
desire to see an officer of high rank or
either the army or the navy who gained

FIRE ALARM SYSTEM.
Superintendent Hewitt Recommends
Its Extension and Improvement.
Superintendent Hewitt of the Fire '
Alarm and Police Telegraph filed his an
nual report with the Board of Supervisors
ypst^rday. and he recommended that pro
vision should be made in the next tax levy
for 300 new fire boxes, the placing of
municipal wires underground, a direct tel
ephone system connecting all department
stations with the central office and new
quarters for the office of the department.
He states that the circuits are danger
ously overloaded and of extreme length,
and more room is needed.
The superintendent reports that he has
ordered an emergency circuit placed in all
hotels, theaters and other public build-
Ings, so arranged that the building may
be left in darkness excepting single lights
over exits and in passageways. They will
be controlled by a switch near the main
entrance and accessible to firemen and
the police. He calls attention to the fact
that this is the only large city in the
United Statps that has no order regu
lating the installation of electric wires
according to the rules of the National
Board of Underwriters. He recommends
the passage of an ordinance empowering
the department to issue certificates under
those rules certifying to the efficiency of
the work done.
During the fiscal year ending June 30
last the department received S2B alarms
and 87.427 police calls. The equipment con
sists of 267 gongs and tappers, 531 miles
of wire. 22?2 cells of battery, 278 fire box
es. 200 police boxes, 43 engine house ser
â¼toes and 5 central police systems. The '
service has been extended to Ocean View,
and, with other extensions, required 257
poles and 70 miles of wire.
Ha estimates that the wiring; of all en
esting talker, and said that when the
troops of his State return the splendid
hospitality shown them by California wlil
be referred to in thousands of Minnesota
homes.

fornia was introduced the banqueters al

R. B. Carpenter responded to the toast
"Our Country." Every one knows what
the country is. he said. The people are
prosperous and happy, which Indicates the
state of existing conditions, although the
soldiers of the country are in active serv
ice against another people. He decried
the efforts of alleged traitors in the
United States to belittle the efforts of
the constituted authorities to carry out
the policy they have planned for the Phil
ippines. What a difference there Is be
tween the United States and France, he
said. In France they are trying an inno
cent man accused of being a traitor, while
in this country they permit a traitor to
go unpunished.
While the committee had done every
thing in its power to make the banquet

quet hall.

Flea fÂ»r Home-Made Cigars.
At a meeting of the Pacific Coast Beer
Bottlers" Protective Association held yes
terday the question of giving encourage
! ment to local cigar-makers was discussed.
I It was stated that there are at the pres
i ent time very few local white men en
] gaged in the manufacturing of cigars, j In
i order to show the sympathy of this as
sociation for the cigar-makers of San
I Francisco the following resolutions were
: adopted:
Whereas, The promising outlook for home
manufactures has encouraged the' cigar
makers Of San Francisco to Inaugurate
a movement for. the rehabilitation of their
Industry In this metropolis, and believing
that there Is no good reason -why. the Cali- .
fornia made cigars should not be restored'
to full popularity in this community, bring
ing to every retail tradesman In San Fran
cisco direct pecuniary benefits and to every
citizen the . Indirect benefits \ that come
from an increasing permanent population
and the steady expansion of our manufac
turing industries, therefore be it
Resolved, That the Pacific Coast Beer
Bottlers' Protective Association hereby
pledges its moral support to the movement
now on foot under the direction of the San
Francisco Protective Association to secure
a fair division of patronage to home made
; cigars, and to do everything In Its power to
I restore to full favor in this community tha
California blue label cigar.

DESTRUCTION OF
THE SATURNUS
Steamer Captured by
Filipinos.
Spectal Dispatch to The CalL
WASHINGTON, Sept. 14.â report has
been received by mail at the War Depart
ment giving an account of the capture |
and destruction of the steamer Saturnus, '
which carried $40,000 in cash and a valu- j
able cargo. The report follows:
The United States gunboat Pampanga ar
rived here yesterday morning bringing news of
the capture of the steamer Saturnus, belonging
to the Compania Maritima. The facts as gath
ered from those who know are as follows:
The Saturnus sailed from Manila on the |
morning of July M for San Fernando de la ]
Union, on the north side of the Gulf or Lin- |
gayan Cayvian. Currlmao and Aparrt. all of
which are northern ports. It is supposed the
vessel arrived off San Fernando on the 31st
ult. When discovered by the gunboat Pam- j
panga she was lying with her nose buried In
the sand of the shore south of the city of San
Fernando. Both anchors were out and smoke,
which hovered closely over the vessel, showed
her to be on fire. Small boats with native
crews could be seen carrying the cargo of the
burning vessel ashore.
Little suspecting the true condition of affairs,
the gunboat Pampanga sent a boat out to the j
burning vessel to investigate, but upon the ap- :
I proach of the boat with her American blue
jackets the natives immediately retired and [
opened fire upon the boat's crew, using Mauser â 
and, Remington rifles from concealed trenches |
and sending shells from two three-Inch guns, :
which formed a concealed battery, over and |
close to the little boat.
The watchful officers on the Pampanga Im- j
mediately ordered the fire from the rebel j
trenches to be returned by the crew of the gun
boat and under cover of the guns of the war
' ship' the boat reached the Saturnus. A brief
I inspection of the vessel showed every Indica
( tion that she had been looted by the rebels,
i but the examination failed to disclose any sign
lof the crew or anything to indicate that a j
' struggle had taken place on board. The boat's
: crew then attempted to raise the anchors, but,
i falling in this, they cut the anchor chains and
i attached a hawser from the Pampanga to the
â¢ propeller of the Saturnus. An attempt was then
made to pull her out of the mud and sand, but
1 was unsuccessful.
The Pampanga then abandoned the wrecked
Saturnus an signaled the Concord, which was
sighted as she was crossing the Gulf of Lin
gayan, and reported all the facts of the dis
covery to her commander. The Pampanga was
thereupon ordered to Manila to report the af
â  fair while the Concord remained to attend the
rebels and the wreck. "When the gunboat left
the scene the flames on the Saturnus had en
veloped the vessel, and she, to all appearances,
was doomed.
The Saturnus was commanded by Captain
1 Antonio Aralueea and had two officers, three
1 engineers and a crew of thirty-six natives. It
' is supposed that all were taken prisoners by the
: rebels.
The carsro of the vessel consisted of 520 cases j
Of petroleum, of ten gallons to the case; 600
demijohns of wine. 143 barrels of wine. SO sacks
of rice, 10 cases of linen cloth. 7 cases of money,
containing $40.00") In cash, and 1496 packages
and bales of various kinds. In addition to the
i cargo the vessel carried twenty-six passengers
i and two children, all of whont were either
Filipinos or Chinese.
I There Is absolutely no truth In the rumor of
the assassination or murder of the crew of the
vessel.

pania General de Tobacco, which sailed a few
days after the Saturnus for the same port, re
turned yesterday with the information that the

It is generally believed that a portion of the

Lively Drawn Battle.
WATERBURT, Conn., Sept. 14.â Dan
Murphy of Waterbury and Australian
Jimmy Ryan fought a twenty-round
draw before the Waterbury Athletic Club, j
In the second round Ryan landed a solar I
plexus blow on Murphy, which caused the j
latter to hang to the ropes. Ryan did not i
follow up his advantage, however. In the j
succeeding round Murphy did the leading, j
landing frequently on th* face and head, j
but In almost every case Ryan came back I
with wicked righthand swing's on Mur- j
phy's wind. In the final round Murphy !
went at his man in hammer and tongs i
fashion and punished him severely.
He could not find the jaw, however, for
I a knockout.
Hart Gets the Decision.
STOUX CITY. lowa. Sept. 14.â ThÂ«
feature of the second night of Tommy
White's fistk: carnival was a fifteen
round battle between Sig Hart and
"Australian" Bill Murphy. Both men
were aggressive, and the battle was hot
from the opening round until the close.
Hart, although a head and a half shorter
than Murphy, who also had a much long
er reach, put up the most scientific fight,
and was awarded the decision on points
at the end of the fifteenth round.
FOUGHT HIS CAPTORS.
Frederick Gilbert, a Maniac, Gives
Four Policemen Battle.
Frederick Gilbert, a lithographer, living
at 1522 O'Farrell street, suddenly became i
insane last night and drove his hand â 
through a window in his home, cutting a ,
deep gash in the wrist. His mother, hear
ing the crash, entered the room occupied
by her son and found him wrapping a
blanket around his wrist. As his actions
denoted that he had suddenly lost his
a, Mrs. Gilbert summoned Police
man Aiken. who started to take him into
custody. Gilbert vigorously resisted his
efforts and a desperate struggle ensued, j
With the assistance of three other police-J
men. who were attracted to the house by
thf> maniacal screams of Gilbert. Aiken
finally bound him with ropes and dragged
him from the house. He was then placed
in tie patrol wagon and driven to the Re
ceiving Hospital. Police Surgeon Heintz, :
who was supposed to be on duty at the
time, could not be found and Dr. Hartley, ;
his former interne, who happened to be
present, was compelled to dress the
wound in Gilberts wrist. He was after
ward removed to the insane department ;
and locked up in a padded cell.
It is claimed that Gilbert was formerly
an inmate of Agnews Asylum for the In- \
sane.

SACRAMENTO FAIR.
Finishes in Eastern Races.
GARRETT COCHRAN WILL
LEAD THEM TO VICTORY
BERKELEY, Sepi U ivrkeley collegians attributed their last football
victory to enthusiasm and to Garret! Cocbiun. If to-day's outburst is
any indication of how the Blue and Gold will fare during the coming
season, Berkeley ought to do great things on the gridiron. Cochran
arrived to-day. The whole university turned out to give him a rous
ing welcome.
The big coach reached Sixteenth-street Station, Oakland, on this morn
ing's Southern Overland train. There his reception was worthy of a football
hero. Over 250 students braved the penalties of being absent from college
exercises and went in a body from the university town to be present at the
depot when Cochran arrived. Their good spirits led them to play tricks with
the Southern Â±-a< irtc. and that corporation is out of 250 douo.e fares which
legitimately belong to it. Once on the train, the men realized themselves
masters of the situation, and, piling into the last car. locked the door and
refused to let in the conductor. That functionary's efforts to collect fares
resulted In his being checkmated at every attempt, and the people at Six
teenth-street Station were surprised to see a carload of students pile out of
the windows of the smoker as soon as the Berkeley local stopped. In a hen
roost near the c -pot the men found a rooster. They decorated the bird with
blue and gold ribb ins, and, as Cochran stepped off hi? train, presented it to
him with cheers and yells.
Cochran was evidently much pleased with his reception. He is looking
in splendid condition, and seems much improved after a year's rest. This
afternoon he made his first appearance upon the football field. Professors
and students lined the- bleachers and joined in the cheering as the big coach
came on the gridiron and shook hands with th< Football men. After a light
practice Cochran declared that Berk-ley had a harder tussle than ever be
fore her this year, and that the men would have to get down to steady
work at once.

BROKEN GAFF OF THE
SHAMROCK BEING FIXED
During the Interim the Yacht Will
Continue Her Trial Spins
With the Old Boom. f
NEW YORK, Sept. 14.â "Will" Fife Jr.
and his associates were busy to-day re
pairing the Shamrock's gaff topsail. The
spars were sent to the yard early in the
morning, and W. D. Dickey, manager of
the plant, was asked to hurry the work
required. The collapsed racing gaff had
to be repaired, as there was no duplicate
of It among the numerous spars sent over
by Mr Fife The yard officials put some
of their best machinists on the Job but
could not promise to complete It for two
or three days.
Under these circumstances, which Mr
Fifo partly anticipated. it was decided to
lengthen the old gaff to the size of the
racing spar, so that the yacht could have
a trial test to-morrow over a logged-off
course. A3 the yacht's practice spins in
American waters have been limited, Mr
tire is now becoming somewhat anxious
and will take advantage of every suita
ble day until the craft is docked to put
her in thorough racing shape.
Both the racing gaff and that used on
the old mainsail are of nickel steel. In the
gaff that collapsed and which is now being
repaired the steel is three-sixteenths of
an inch In thickness. This gaff is sixty
seven feet, long to an inch, which, al
though formidable enough, is five feet
less than experts and wiseacres had es
timated it.. As the old gaff was sixty
three feet ten inches long, the addition
given to it is three feet two inches. These
figures give an inkling of the extent to
which Mr. Fife has increased the area
of the. Shamrock's mainsail since she was
sailed with the cutter Britannia.
Both gaffs are made after the manner
of an old-fashioned stovepipe, and nave
no Inside bracing.

BIG COURSING EVENTS.
Franey's Death an Accident.
NEW RACE
PARK FOR
THE CAPITAL
Wealthy Citizens of Sacra-
mento Plan to Purchase a
Fin* Tract of Land.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. 14.â Sacramento's
citizens are wildly enthusiastic over the
success of the State Fair of 1*99. They
have just realized that success can be
achieved by a display of concerted en
ergy. The citizens formed a State Fair
Club and decided to inject liberal supplies
of ginger into the annual exposition. Old
methods were discarded and the result
has been decidedly gratifying; in fact, it
has exceeded the wildest dream of the
most enthusiastic promoters.
The citizens have been fearful for some
months past that they might lose the
State Fair, as there lias been a hitch in
the efforts to dispose of tlie present park,
which is located almost fn the heart of
the city and which has grown too small
for the purpose* for which it, is intended.
The Legislature gave authority to sell the
present park and purchase a new one, but
for .some reason. there was a hitch, and
the people had about made up their minds
that nothing would be donee- â¢ - â  :
The Call's correspondent learned, to
night of a project which has been.inaugu
rated by some of the wealthiest citizens
of Sacramento, which will, if carried out,
give to California's- capital one of .the
finest racing parks on me Pacific Coast.
The plan of the projectors is to purchase
a great tract of land near the city, put
up modern clubhouses and grandstands,
construct a track and stables, pay off the
indebtedness of the State Agricultural
Society and turn the new park over to the
society in exchange for â the , present
grounds.' The indebtedness is about $29,000.
The citizens who are working upon this
scheme are not in it for selfish .motives.
They have the interests of the. city at
heart. They intend, in the event of the
success of their plans, to ' convert ,, the
present racing grounds into a bedutlful
driving park. Old fences and buildings
will be torn down and trees, flowers and
shrubs will be planted. A mile track will
be maintained for pleasure driving and
there will be roadways where parties may |
turn out or drive out at quarter or half
mile points. The scheme is meeting with
great encouragement and will no . doubt
be carried out.

SACRAMENTO
DAY AT THE
FAIR RACES
Admiral Kautz and General
Shatter Are Cheered on
Their Arrival.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Sept. 14.â The wealth
and fashion of Sacramento turned out in
honor of Sacramento day at the races
this afternoon, the immense grandstand
being packed and the betting ring a surg
ing mass <if people. The day was suffo
catingly hot with but one chilling blast
to cool the oppressive atmosphere. Short
ly after 2 o'clock the clarion voice of
Caller Dinue announced through a mega
i phone the approaching arrival of the
I Governor and staff, also General Shatter
and Admiral Kautz. guests of the occa
sion. Escorted by Troop B, Cavalry, the
vehicles containing the distinguished vis
itors paraded before the stand, the band
struck up a national air and strangely
enough the crowd refused to enthuse.
Even when three cheers were proposed
for his excellency but two or three hol
low, cracked and clammy voices respond
ed, 'the southern Governor is not very
I popular in this northern part of the State.
j General Shatter graciously responded by
raising his cap in acknowledgment of
the hearty welcome accorded him.
The racing was no different from any
other day, four favorites meeting with
defeat, and the books drawing an eagle
bird. Neernut, rank and unsteady, was a
bitter disappointment in the 2:13 trot,
which went to Galttte Wilkes in straight
heats. The Autumn handicap for two
year-olds over a mile fell to Bathos, su
perbly ridden by Frank Duffy. Cromwell
defeated a small field for the Capital City
stake. .
In the event for Sacramento County
roadsters, Regina F, driven by "Billy"
Lampert, won, the mare proving oil from
the can. Peo, over which F. E. Wright
held the reins, ruled a slight favorite
over Regina in the opening betting, but
the mare headed him throughout in each
hfat. Harry, driven by Clarence Nelson,
and Lady, with A. \V. O'Brien in the bike,
had the red bunting swished in their faces
the first heat.
A nicely balanced field of eight scored
for the word in the 2:13 class trot, with
Neernut a commanding favorite. The San
ta Ana horse acted cranky in the first
heat and was beaten hands down by Ga
lette Wilkes, driven by Charley Durfee.
Neernut was even a stronger choice for
the next heat, but the brown mare again
repeated the trick without a bobble in
2:14%, This time Hazel Klnney was sec
ond and Claudius third.
The first running event left a deep dark
brown taste in the mouths of many spec
tators. It was a six-furlong sprint, with
Amasa heavily played at odd? of 4 to 5
throughout. Of the others Yule, with
Butler up, seemed to be the only one try
ing. Mafada, Novia and Mike Rice could
all three have beaten the winner.
Mat Storn thought very highly of the
chances of his youngster Sunello for the
Autumn handicap, and the public coin
sent him to the post favorite. Loullier
rode one of his bungling races, finally
ending up In the show. Duffy, astride
Bathos, a 5 to 1 chance, stole away in the !
lead, and in a drive at the end led Bogus
Bill past the judges over a length in I:4BVs-
The Capital City stake, at one mile and
a furlong, was gathered in by Cromwell,
the even money choice, piloted by Maek
lin. Monrovia cut out the running for
nearly a mile, when she was collared and
beaten out five lengths. Grady disposed
of Mlstleton for the show. ,
Flush of Gold was plunged on to win
the five-furlong dash for two-year-olds,
but was slow to leave the post, and
Druidess won al! the way. The favorite
came with a rush, leading out Rose of
Hilo for the place.
A six-furlong selling run closed the
afternoon's sport- Donator. well ridden
by Morse and a mild favorite in the ring,
tame from behind, and in a hard drive
downed the 15 to 1 shot. Somis. a head.
Negligence, after making the running,
died away, just managing to get the
show.

FOOTBALL
PRACTICE IS
COMMENCED
Coach Chamberlin at Stan
ford Gives the Men Their
First Lesson.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, Sept. 14.â
Between forty and fift<- football men were
out last Tuesday and received their first
lesson from Coach Chamberlin. He is
quite pleased with the material at his dis
posal. The only fault he has to find is
that they are mostly light. But this lack
wi!l be remedied when. "Babe" Burnett,
Cairns, De Forest, Dole, Wrigley and
Leavitt appear on the field. Another man
who may do some great work for Stan
ford is Christy, a two hundred pounder
j from Los Angeles, who has had three
years' experience as guard on the Uni-
I versity 01 Southern California team. De
I Forest of Portland is another man with
I a good football record who is expected.
> The rtturn of the First California Regi
i ment brings back besides Burnett, the
I great '97 center, "Guiby" Dole, guard on
I the 'Ol's freshman team; Leavitt, sub end
on the '91 'varsity, and Wrigley, a sub
guard the same year.
There is an abundance of material for
the freshman team. At present it is im
â  possible to make any forecast of what
j the personnel of the team will be. "Jack"
Reynolds and Lewers, old 'varsity heroes
of the '95 team, now of the law faculty,
will devote much time to the baby team,
thus relieving Chamberlin of this work.
Some of the more promising candidates
as they have shown up in practice so far
are Traeger and Lee for line positions,
P. Parker, John, Grider and Campbell for
halfs. Geer, Thayer and Roberts are
out for full backs, with the chances prob
ably even. For quarter, Luck, captain of
the West Aurora. 111.. High School team,
is the most promising candidate. With
the exception of center, there are plenty
of candidates for the other positions.
Another week will mark a great advance
ment on the gridiron at Stanford, as the
coach is hero at work and all the men
have suits who have applied for them.

10th 4 Â»W>DDf> T (tku. M*iÂ» Â»â ) OAKUNOGOV. PINGREE IS
AGAINST TRUSTS
In Favor of Their Com
plete Annihilation.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CHICAGO. Sept. 14.â Enthusiastic inter
est and a crowded hall put to-night's
session of the conference on trusts sharp
ly in contra.--, with the forenoon and aft
ernoon gatherings. Central Music Hall
was packed with an audience that repre
sented, nearly every class of people. Gov
ernor Pir.gree of Michigan was the star
attraction. The Governor was received
with applause so fervent that it was some
rr.lr.utes tx fore he could proceed with hfs
address. He was obliged to rise several
times after taking his seat and bow ac
knowledgment before the audience would
allow the chairman to introduce the next
speaker.
The house was divided against Itself
during- the address of the next speaker
ex-Secretary of the Treasury Charles
Foster. The audience finally became so
demonstrative In interrupting the speaker
with questions and remarks that a dele
gate asked the chairman to clear the gal
leries unless the Interruption ceased.
Governor Pingree's subject was "Th?
Effect of Trusts on Our National. Life and
Citizenship."
He began by mildly criticizing the Civic
ration of Chicago, declaring that ir
all of the sixty-nine questions distributed
by the Federation broadcast over the
country, as a means of gathering mater
ia.l for the use of the conierence. only om
question related in any way to the effect
of trusts upon society. He deplored thÂ«
fact that in all the discussion, business
considerations â the almighty dollar
seemed to be uppermost.
While it is a popular fallacy to denj
the existence of social classes in this
country, he said. if Is nevertheless In
disputable that well defined classes di
vide our society. The strength of the re
public, he said, rests in the great mid
die class, the manufacturers, jobbers
middle men, commercial travelers anc
business men generally and it would bÂ«
little short of calamity to encourage an>
industrial development that would affect
unfavorably this Important class. Close
to them and another strong element art
the skilled mechanics and artisans.
It was the effect of the trust upon the
classes. Governor Pir.gree maintained,
that should be first considered. As he
viewed the situation, the tendency of thÂ«
trusts was to drive these men into th*
service of the great combinations, where
their identity would be lost and where
they would become mere cogs and little
wheels in a great complicated machine.
Ambition ana perhaps inventive genius
would be deadened and killed, inde
pendence would be lost and a sort of in
dustrial slavery would follow. Commer
cial feudalism, "he said, is the logical out
come of the trust, the trust manager be
coming the feudal baron. Commercial
advantages and -advantages might be
argued, "he said, but it would not be
honest to the people either in this con
ference or in legislative assemblies, il
the effect of the trust upon our national
life <t.rA citizenship be not considered.
Governor Pingree believed it would be
better to be forever poor, but indepen
dent and happy as individuals, than tc
lay the foundations for industrial slaver}
and tyranny- Equality of opportunity tc
all mÂ»-n would be better than the control
of the worid's trade.
"I. favor complete and prompt anni
hilation of the trust." declared Governoi
Pingree, "with due regard for propertj
rights, of course."

RAILROAD DIRECTORS
HONOR THE MEMORY OF
CORNELIUS VANDERBILT
NETV YORK, Sept. 14.â The directors of I
:he nine Vanderbilt railroads met togeth- ,
;r yesterday at the Grand Central station
and adopted appropriate resolutions on
the death of Cornelius Vanderbilt.
The New York Central, Chicago and
Northwestern, New fork and Harlem,
Lake Shore and Michigan Southern, Mich
igan Central. Canada Southern. New
York. Chicago and St. Louis: Cleveland,
Cincinnati. Chicago and St. Louis and the
Chicago. St. Paul. Minneapolis and Omaha
were represented.
J. Pierpont Morgan presided, and resolu
tions drawn up by Chauncey M. Depew,
Marvin Hughitt and D. O. Mills were pre
sented. It was directed that the report be
placed on the records of the several com
panies and that a copy of it be sent to
the family of the deceased. The resolu- .
The structure that will receive the remains of Cornelius Vanderbilt and others of the family that follow him for
a. couple of generations to come stands near the little town of Newdorp, Staten Island. It is just outside of the Mora
vian Cemetery, where stands the family vault in which the "Old Commodore" was laid to rest, and near the farmhouse â¦
In which W. H. Vanderbilt lived and where Cornelius J. and others of his children were born. *>
It was begun in ISB4, and was not quite completed at the time of the death of TV*. H. Vanderbilt. December S, I*vs. 4
It was intended to take the place of the ojd Vanderbilt family vault, which was of scant accommodation and rather in-
secure. It was designed and erected by the celebrated architect. Richard M. Hunt, at a cost of 1900,000. The style of
the tomb is Romanesque throughout, and the whole structure contrasts strongly with the simple vault surmounted by
- mite shaft thirty feet high, which served as a tomb for the founder of the Vanderbilt fortunesâ the only ornamen- <
tation of which is the name VAN DERBILT over the door of the vault. â¢ â¦

DESTROYED CULVERT PIPES.
Dug TJp by Workmen Who Thought
They Were Discarded Sewers.
Tn excavating for the foundation of the
new car tracks on Montgomery street the
it men employed have allowed noth
ing to block their way. At the intersec
."' \tp mery and Bush streets the
excavations were somewhat deeper than
at other points along the line, and ab..ut
feet below the grade of the street a
culvert pipe was uncovered. It led from
the culvert at the Mills building to the
sewer running through Bush street.
As the pipe interfered somewhat with
instruction of the car tracks the
workmen had no hesitation in removing
,:>le of sections and plugging up the
exposed ends with concrete. The excuse
for r-r.dering the culvert pipe useless was
that it was thought to be an old sewer,
and as it was dry the workmen did not
think it was of any use. so they complet
ed the job by destroying it.
The matter would not have attracted
any attention until the rainy season had
not the business men of the vicinity no
ticed what the workmen were doing and
protested, but to no avail.
The attention of the Street Superintend
ent was called to the destruction of the
pipe, and he at once stated that h*
would make a thorough ini-estigation and
would force the Market-street Railway
Company to repair whatever injury U3
workmen had done.
OTA WILL LAND
The Secretary of the Treasury De
cides That He Is Not an
Alien Immigrant.
Deputy Immigration Commissioner
Schell received yesterday a telegram from
Commissioner-General Powderly. indorsed
by Secretary Gage, instructing Captain
Schell to allow S. Ota to land. The tele
gram stated that a letter of explanation
would follow by mail. Ota was rÂ»!<>asÂ»d
at once and took the earliest opportunity
to thank Count Nutsu, the Japanese Con
sul, and W. H. L,. Barnes fur their suc
cessful efforts in having him landed over
the head of the local immigration offi
cers.
Ota has been a merchant in this city for
eight y^ars. He went to Japan last March
to purchase iWO worth of goods for his
store. When he returned about a month
ago he was denied a landing on the
ground that he was an alien immigrant
and afflicted with a disease.
The matter was taken before Judge de
Haven, and he decided that Ota was not
an alien immigrant, that he had acquired
a domicile here and was therefore entitled
to land, but that the court had no Juris
diction to review the act of the Immigra
tion Commissioner. The case was* then
laid before the Secretary of tHe Treasury,
who, after examining the facts, revoked
the action of the local Commissioner.
SEWERS TO BE REPAIRED.
The Street Committee of the Board of
Supervisors at a meeting yesterday re
ported In favor of the reconstruction of
the sewers in Sixth street between How
ard and Bryant and the adjacent sew
ers and also of the sewer in Harrison
street between Fifth and Sixth
which have all been found to be in a bad
condition. The sum of $20,000 will be pro
vided for In the next tax levy for the pur
pose.
The construction of a bridge over the
tracks of the Southern Pacific Company
on San Jose avenue, near Mountain ave
nue was also recommended and the sum
of $13,000 will be set apart for the purpose.
The City Engineer was directed to make
the- necessary surveys and prepare plans
and specifications at a cost of $495.

State. The purpose of the gathering is

State and national governments to survey

' for use in dry seasons and for Irrigation
j purposes. Also for discussing the best

tion of young forests. At a meeting of

men."

HURRICANE IN
WEST INDIES
Eight Hundred People
Left Homeless.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
ST. THOMAS, D. V- X, Sept. 14.âAd
vices received -at St. Kitts to-day from
the inland of Anguilla, one of the Brit
ish West India islands. Leeward group,
say that a hurricane during the night
of September 8 destroyed 200 houses and
rendered 800 people homeless.
There was considerable loss of prop
erty and similar damage at St. Martin.
MADDEN ON HIS DEFENSE.
The Teamster Tells a Plain Story
About His Connection With
Ruby Raymond.
John F. Madden, the teamster for E.
Martin & Co., wholesale liquor dealers,
who was charged with attempting to'
"fix" the case of Ruby Raymond, the
: woman from Sari Jose accused of taking
two girls, Daisy Miller and Nathalie Dan
forth, under Is years of age. to San Jose
I for Immoral purposes, appeared before
: Judge Mogan yesterday.
Madden told a straightforward story.
i He said he had received a letter from a
! friend in San Jose to help the woman in
I securing an attorney to help her. He
I knew Mack, and when Ruby called at his
; house Monday night he went with her to
Mucks office. There she gave him $150 as
: part payment of his fee to defend her.
Nothing was ever said about "fixing" the
! case, and if the defendant said otherwise
she lied.
The Judge asked that the woman should
be placed on the stand, but Mack objected
i to her testifying, as she was a defendant.
The Judge was indignant, and said that
if anybody had tampered with the case
he would punish him and would also
1 take the matter before the Grand Jury.
! He did not like the assertion that any one
could "fix" a case in his court, and he
was determined to sift the -matter to the
bottom. He ordered Madden to be in at
, tendance in his court tiil the case against
j the woman Raymond was disposed of.
; The case will come up this morning.
SAM BEAUNHART IN COURT.
He Is Instructed and Arraigned on
the Perjury Charge and the
Case Is Continued.
Samuel Braunhart, Port Warden, was
nervous and excited in Judge Graham's
court yesterday as he sat alongside of hia
attorney, Garret McEnerney. to answer to
the charge of perjury preferred against
him by ex-Senator Dunn.
When the case was called he stood up
and was Instructed and arraigned by the j
Judge. McEnerney at once asked that an [
immediate hearing of the case should be :
had, but as he had to attend to a case
in Judge Coffey's equrt he asked that the !
-nould go on in the evening.
Prosecuting Attorney Wentworth said
he knew nothing about the facts in the j
case, as he believed that special counsel
bad been engaged to prosecute. He was
therefore unprepared to proceed.
At this stage Attorney McGregor ap
peared and Bald that Senator McGowan
bad been engaged to conduct the prosecu
tion, but as he was engaged In one of trte
Superior Courts he would ask fur a con
tinuance.
Dunn was called upon and in answer to
McEnerney said that McGowan could not
be present as he was engaged elsewhere. |
He had talked with him, and he had said j
that he would prosecute the case.
After further discussion the Judge in- i
timated that he would centinue the case |
till this morning, and if agreeable to the
prosecution he Would tix tne hearing for
this evening.
AT THE CHUTES
High. Divers Harmon and Seabuxy
Create a Sensation by Their
Daring Feats.
There was a large attendance at tne
Chutes last night, the principal attraction
being the high diving of Harry Harmon
and S. Forest Seabury, who are said to
be the champions of the world at that
particular form of athletic exercise.
A tdatform had been erected about .85
feet high near the scenic railway depot,
and from its top the two men performed
what is called the layout dive and did it
to the satisfaction and admiration of the
hundreds of spectators, performing a
somersault in their long descent into the
tank, which was about v eight feet deep.
They were loudly cheered for their dar
ing and clever act.
Besides the divers there was the usual
amateur show and the crowds that filled,
the theater were well rewarded for their
time and patience. One of the chief fea
tures was the fat women's boxing contest
and it afforded the greatest amusement
to the spectators. Adgie and her per
forming lions were also a great attrac
tion.

RETURNED TO SAN JOSE.
Police Believe That Wong Hing, the
Murderer, Has Deserted
This City.
The police have been working for the
past few days endeavoring to apprehend
Wong Hlng, the San Jose criminal who
fired three shots at Ah Fong and Ah
Yum in Chinatown on Tuesday afternoon.
They have searched the Chinese quarter
from end to end. but the wily shooter has
disappeared as mysteriously as he did
after using his revolver on Lee Ong Poon
and Deputy Sheriff Mackay in San Jose.
Sergeant Duke and posse, -who have
been engaged in the search, discovered
that Hing had entered a building on
Spofford alley, where the shooting oc
curred, in which are contained a number
of highbinder headquarters, and passed
out over a roof to Waverly place, and
made his escape. The officers have come
to the conclusion that the man has re
turned to San Jose and have notified the
police of the Garden City, who will keep
a watch .for him. â  .

DEAD FOR THREE DAYS.
John Cunningham, who was at one time
a capitalist in San Francisco, was found
dead in his room by neighbors about 6
o'clock last evening. The man had evi
dently been dead for about three days.
Dr. Potts, who attended the deceased,
says that he had been suffering from
heart failure for some time and is not
surprised at the fact that he was found
dead. Cunningham formerly owned ex
tensive property in this city, but the last
piece and the house in which he lived was
recently mortgaged to a bank for $12.00u.
It was about to be sold, after which the
man, who was 71 years of age, would have
been left penniless.
Reception to Y. M. I. Volunteers.
The members of the Young Men's Insti
tute among the California Volunteers wiil
be banqueted by the board of manage
ment. Y. M. L, at Pioneer Hall next Sat
urday evening.
Covers will be laid for 400. Besides the
volunteers, the invited guests will include
the Governor. Mayor Phelan and other
prominent city and State officials, the
supreme president of the organization
and prominent people in local church and
institute circles. Rev. Father McKinnon
and Mayor Phelan will be among the
speakers". The banquet will be preceded
by a grand concert.

TRYING TO CLEAR OFF
THE MERCANTILE`S DEBT
Huntington's Donationâ A Circular
Asking Aid of Subscribers to
; Increase Membership.
The Mercantile Library Association is
rapidly paying off the debt of the library
and expects that the institution will be
on a paying basis in a short time. The
last donation made to the association was
a check for $2000, the gift of C. P. Hunt
ington. With a few more donations of
this amount the entire debt will have be
come liquidated and every dollar received
thereafter will be expended in improving
the library- The association has also
issued a circular to every subscriber re
questing that he use his influence in se
curing two additional members, and in
this manner the Library Association may
become one of the strongest in the West.
C P,Huntington proffered the donation
to President Newhall some time ago and
the money was paid over yesterday. The
following communication was received
from New York directing the treasurer of
the Southern Pacific Company to advance
the amount of the donation:
Southern Pacific Company, Office of the Presi
dent, 23 Broad Street. New York, Septem
ber 8, 1599. . _, Â
Mr N ' T Smith, Treasurer Southern Pacific
Company. San Francisco, Cal.: Dear Sirâ
presentation of this order please pay to Mr.
George A. Newhall. president, or to the treas
urer of the Mercantile Library Association of
San Francisco, J two thousand dollars (J2COO),
and charge to my **Â£"*;Â£"!s&Â£%"
The following circular has been issued
to all members of the association:
Dear Sirâ As a member of the Mercantile
Library Association interested in its welfare.
we ask if it is not possible for you to secure
two new members for the association.
We inclose herewith two blank applications
for membership and a stamped envelope in
which to return the new subscriptions secured.
A concerted effort on the part of the mem
bers should increase our membership from its
present number of 700 to over WOOâ which, with
the contributions now .being received, will
quickly restore the association, with its mag
nificent library of 74,000 volumes, to a most
prosperous condition. â¢
The management will most cordially appre
ciate such consideration on your part. Yours
BinCere GEORGE A. NEWHALL. President.,
The circular is expected to bring forth
excellent results and many members have
signified 'their intention of working ener
getically to realize for the association its
expectations. The life membership will
also be largely increased and ten appli
cations for life certificates have been re
ceived These will be issued as soon as
the number of applications reaches twen
ty-five. The entire debt of the association
originally amounted to $60,000. .of which
about $10 000 has already been secured.
Obed Horr has been appointed assistant
to the president and is making a vigorous
effort 'to reduce the Indebtedness.

THE TRANSVAAL.
THE proposition that our Government offer
mediation between England and the Boers
meets with no official favor. It serves, how
r, to bring the actual relation existing between
. and and that republic into general notice. The
tish Government has artfully concealed the nature
extent ot its suzerainty. By the treaty between
Great Britain and the Transvaal it was arranged that
in all external, international matters England
should be suzerain. This relation came up in the
of the Jameson raid. Several American citizens
were involved in that ambitious venture. They ap
d to our Government for relief, and we at once
referred their cases to Great Britain as the suzerain
â er in all international affairs. We received the
urance that the laws of the Transvaal should be
fairly enforced on the trial of our citizens and that
â¢uld be no denial of justice. The domestic
trnment of the republic proceeded to arraign
try the Americans. They were given every de
fensive advantage found in the local laws, were found
â y and sentenced to death. That sentence could
n executed, regardless of any protest and-in
dependent of any interference by this Government or
Great Britain. President Kruger commuted the death
sentence, and as a substitute a very heavy fine was
imposed and paid, and the men were set free and
rted from the country.
In a still more striking way England asknowledged
the absolute internal independence of the Transvaal
by condemning Rhodes for instigating the Jameson
raid and backing the Uitlanders in their revolutionary
proceedings.
Now, however, England has permitted enlargement
that her treaty rights are not limited to
suzerainty in international matters, but that she has
the power to dictate to the Boers in their internal
jMivernment and to demand that they give to British
subjects a vote and voice in the government of the
republic, without relinquishment of their allegiance
to Great Britain. She possesses no such right, and
were the Transvaal her equal in strength .would be far
from claiming it. Nothing but her superior might is
the motive for the making of a claim so absurdly for
eign to her treaty stipulations. But, no matter to
what extent she exceeds her just powers, we are in no
position to protest or to mediate. We have recently
. appeared in the field of exterior politics. We have
gone beyond our own shores, and have as a precedent
action made certain pledges to the world as to our
purposes. Those pledges in regard to Cuba and the
late possessions of Spain remain unfulfilled, and in
instances have been specifically violated. There is
in this country a vicious and carefully propagated
sentiment in favor of violating them all. England
has from the first ingeniously lured us into this posi
tion, for the reason that before we occupied it we
were the only nation that could forcibly and con
sistently protest to her in favor of the wronged and
the weak. We had no beam in our own eye, and had
the right to see the mote in hers. All this is changed
and our right of challenge of another Government is
subject to its right to offer interference in our own
dealings of a similar nature.
We have ceased to be the one power in the world
which, doing equity, could demand it. The change
that has come in the world's affairs since our change
of front and purpose is very significant and appalling
to the weaker peoples and powers. It has propa
gated the notion that a few strong nations have" the
right to divide the world among them, and that it
shall be divide, 1 , up among the United States, Eng
land, Russia and Germany.
Bonaparte set out to make the Continent all
French, and declared that it was destiny that it should
be French or Cossack. His idea was only a little
more monopolistic than the current cult. It is to be
regretted that we have abandoned our moral position
and that the nations of this hemisphere begin to look
upon us with the same suspicion and aversion that
have been felt against England for generations.
There appears to be now a chance that war between
England and the Boers may be averted, but only by
a surrender to Great Britain in matters that she his
no right to control and by concessions so offensive
that they are only yielded in the face of overwhelming
and destructive force.
And so Edna Wallace Hopper, who. since her di
vorce from the fickle Dc Wolf, is known among her
fi ; r;:rls as the "grass Hopper," is not to marry Jockey
Tod Sloan. Mamma Wallace says "Never!"
