PHILIPPINE NEWS.
THE statement by Commissioner Schurman, an.d
the interview with General Reeve published |
exclusively in The Call, give some information j
: of genuine value to the American people. Schurman
affirms the advanced civilization of the Filipinos and ,
their industrial capacity. He professes great conli
dence in the people and much sympathy for their as
pirations. His statement presents certain contradic- |
dictions, due to the pressure of his official undertak
ing. He finds great difficulty in the way of their in
dependence because they are not a nation.
When our forefathers revolted against .England
I they were not a nation. The thirteen colonies had lio
1 i tie except a common racial origin. Each existed
1 j under a separate Royal Charter granted by the Sov
-1 ! ereign of England. They united only in the desire
for independence and self-government, and had no !
common civil bond until after the revolution sue- .
ceeded. That bond, in the old Confederation, was j
very slight and did not create a nation. That act was ;
deferred for six years after the treaty of peace. We I
did not establish nationality and a common Federal j
authority without rebellion, resistance and outbreaks, j
and the issues arising in the national interpretation |
of the Federal constitution were not finally settled
until we had fought the most sanguinary civil -war
ever known, seventy-two years after we attempted to
form a more perfect nationality under the constitu
tion. In the light of our own history Professor
Schurman's objection to Philippine nationality be- !
cause it is not a nation now is very considerably j
modified. His ascription to the people of the quali- j
ties of intelligence, fidelity and virtue is an answer j
to the persistent attempt to represent them as unde
serving savages.
General Reeve makes a still more important con- [
tribution to the stock of information on the subject, j
He found the Filipinos enlightened, orderly, indus
i trious and ambitious. They were modest in their as
i piration for self-government and desirous of. the
suzerainty of the United States in their international |
affairs, while in their domestic concerns they desired j
! I independence and self-povernmtnt. He affirms that
â¢ throughout Luzon. Panay, Negros and Cebu the
, native government preserved order and ruled justly
from the time they conquested those islands, a year I
ago last July. His emphatic language on this subject j
should smite the truth into the conscience of those j
editors who are calling them bandits.
The fact appears proved that no conquerors ever
took their victory with more temperance and absten
tion from outrage on private and property rights than
J these Filipinos, to whom, under the laws of nations \
and of war, the sovereignty of the islands passed by
conquest more than a year ago.
General Reeve is emphatic in the opinion that the
present war of conquest was unnecessary. Looking
at the general facts of the situation this opinion is
j justified. We have treated with the Mahometan Sul- j
' tan o-f the Stilus and have paid him a salary to ac
-1 knowledge our sovereignty while we have left the in
! ternal administration of his country untouched. In
( other words, we have agreed that the Mahometan
Stilus are qualified for domestic independence and
self-government. In the case of the millions of
Christians on the islands of Luzon, Panay, Negros j
and Cebu. we by proclamation denied them the same
rights so freely conceded, with a salary on the side,
to the Mahometans. This places us in the attitude
df claiming that centuries of Christianity have di>- ,
qualified the Christian Malays for self-government, ]
while centuries of Itdam have qualified the Mahom- I
i etan Malays for self-government!
Throughout the East the preference given to
i Mahometanism by the United States will have its
j effect. To our mind no more serious blow could be I
struck at the efforts of Christian missionaries in Asia.
Police Surgeon Heintz seems to be a sensitive
plant from the backwoods. He has posted a bulletin
warning reporters away from the Receiving Hospital
operating-room. The suspicion is well grounded that
Heintz fears that his methods are not up to the metro
politan standard.
THE SHAMROCK TRIAL RUNS.
WHILE the Columbia continues to be a strong
favorite in betting circles, the reports that
come from New York concerning the Sham
rock tend to the conclusion that the American yacht
has by no means such a sure thing as one would sup
pose from the reports of the odds offered by her sup-
J. porters.
The results of the trial trips made by the Shamrock
show that she will probably give the Columbia a hard
race in almost any kind of weather, and that with
strong winds she will have a very good chance to win
if she be properly handled. Commenting upon her ap
pearance and performances the New York Sun says:
"This and that about the Shamrock have produced
the impression that she is more of a novelty than her
predecessors, and that the strong hope unquestionablj
cherished by her owner is that the new ideas in her
will work. She carries the greatest of rigs to drive
her in case the air is light, and, it is believed, the
heaviest of weights at the bottom of the deepest of
; keels to hold her sails up to the wind in case it blows.
As an attempt at np all-round boat she outstrips all
for boldness in design."
It is to be noted, moreover, that those who watched
the trials of the challenger speak in admiration of the
skill pf her captain and her crew. It is therefore not
likely we shall be able to profit by any great su
periority in that respect. Taken altogether the con
clusion from the reports must be that the two yachts
are not far from an equality of merit, and that the
reason why such odds are given against the challenger
is that the American is a bolder bettor than the Briton
and is in this instance betting very largely on pa
triotism.
An unclean bird would be a strikingly appropriate
advertising sign for the Oakland undertakers who,
more than once, have fought like human buzzards for
the possession of the bodies of the dead.
A SIGN OF PROSPERITY.
REPORTS from Chicago are to the effect that
passenger traffic on Western roads centering in
that city has reached a point where it surpasses
the World's Fair records. It is stated that several
times during the past few weeks the stations of
Chicago have received larger crowds of passengers
than on any single day of the great fair, not excepting
those which were made specially attractive by some
conspicuous event of the exposition.
Furthermore, it is stated the large traffic is not due
to excursion or suburban travel, but to the passenger
lists of the regular trains. According to the report:
"Roads like the Grand Trunk are now running their
regular trains in two sections into Chicago. These
trains are running with an nverage of seventy passen
gers to a coach, and some in the aisles in many in
stances. Saturday night at the Polk-street depot, be
tween 8 and n o'clock, over 3000 passengers passed
through the gates. The same crush is visible at the
Union station. The, east and west roads have ajl the
THE SAIS FRANCISCO CALL, FRIDAY, SEPTEMBER 15. 1899.
passenger business they can comfortably handle. Be
tween 20.000 and 25,000 passengers a day was
considered a comfortable crowd at the Union station
in 1893, but as many are now being handled, and on
days of special trains the past records are surpassed."
Of course the explanation of this immense. passen
ger travel, whi(jh is noted in other large cities of the
East as well as in Chicago, is the prevailing prosper
ity of the country- The people this year can. afford
to travel. Residents of the cities have had vacations
in the country, and the farmers with their families are
visiting the cities. The crops have been good, prices
are fair, business is booming, and a general spirit of
satisfaction prevails.
There are good reasons for believing the large pas
senger travel to and from the pleasure resorts and
metropolitan centers of the East during the. sum
mer and the fall will be followed by a correspond
ingly large travel westward to California and south
ward to Florida later in the season. The winter re
sorts of the country will be as well patronized as the
summer resorts have been. The outlook for the
tourist trade of the coming season is therefore excel
lent, and it is probable our noted hotels will do a
record-breaking business.
With such prospects ahead it will be worth while
for Californians to be more than usually energetic
in advertising the attractiveness of the various sec
tions of the State. While this is decidedly the best
winter climate in the United States, the State is to
some extent handicapped by its remoteness from the
centers of population in the East and by the lavish
advertising given to the Florida resorts by the big
companies which own the hotels there. It will prob
ably pay us well this winter to make special efforts
to attract tourists. When passenger travel is sur
passing the records of the World's Fair year at
Chicago it is time to turn it in our direction if pos
sible.
An irritable bill collector of San Rafael has indi
cated an unkind desire to perforate several news
paper men who have angered him. He should not
forget that jaw practice never perforates.
r~X IRI'XTORS Gedge and Conlon, members of
; ) the Supply Committee of the Board of Edu
â cation, took occasion at the meeting of the
board on Wednesday evening to deny that there had
been any fraud or corrupt collusion between either of
them and any bidder for the contracts for furnishing
the school supplies.
The denial of Director Conlon was made with the
indefinite fluency of extempore speech, but that of j
Director Gedge was more orderly and formal, being
expressed in a written communication denying that he
had received $2000 for assisting in the award of con
tracts to certain firms which he named, and denying
hlso having had the specifications changed. He sub- |
mitted letters from the firms' named, and also one)
from the secretary of the board, supporting his deniab. j
His communication closed with a request that the I
board investigate the charges. After some consider- |
ation the board decided the investigation could, be j
better made by the Grand Jury, and accordingly a j
resolution' was adopted requesting that body to in- ;
vestigate and pledging the board to offer the Grand !
Jury every facility to get at the truth of the whole \
matter.
The action taken by the board is right. An inves- |
tigation by the board itself would not be satisfactory
to the people. The matter is one that should be in
quired into by the Grand Jury â a body that can
gather information given under oath, that has author- [
ity to bring indictments against the accused parties if
found guilty. Moreover, the Grand Jury is an im
partial body, while the Board of Education is too
closely identified with its own Committee on Sup
plies to be properly fitted for conducting such an ex
amination as is required in this case.
The Call has made it known that Director Gedge
informed at least one reputable citizen that the sum
of $2000 was paid to him for conceding certain advan
tages to a firm of bidders for the contract for fur- [
nishing school supplies, and also that he had given
$800 of the money to Director Conlon, who. accord
ing to the story told by Genge, was a party to the deal.
Such is the evidence now before the public. An
investigation is imperative, but the Board of Educa
tion has done well in referring the matter to the
Grand Jury. That is the right course.
COLOR LINE IN POLITICS.
QNE of the State campaigns now under way
which is of national importance is that in
North Carolina, where an attempt is being
made to bring about the adoption of an amendment
to the constitution which will virtually eliminate the
black vote of the State for the rest of this generation.
The proposed amendment requires an educational
qualification for the right of suffrage, but it exempts
from the requirement all voters who are lineal
descendants of men who were voters in the United
States prior to 1867. The exemption, it will be seen,
includes all white men, but bars negroes. Conse
quently the illiterate white will continue to be a voter
even if the amendment be adopted, but the illiterate
negro will be deprived of suffrage.
The plan is similar to that already adopted ill
Mississippi and South Carolina. It is a part of a
movement which has been spreading throughout the
Southern States, and may yet be adopted by all of
them. It is said the people of Virginia and West
Virginia- and even those of Maryland are giving the
subject much consideration, and success in North
Carolina may lead to the adoption of the plan in
those States.
According to reports from North Carolina it ap
pears the amendment will be carried by a sweeping
majority. Strangely enough a considerable number
of white Republicans are said to be in favor of it", and
one of the ablest of their leaders, Thomas Settle, an
ex-Representative in Congress, is quoted as having
declared it to be "the one and wisest solution of the
most difficult problem now confronting the people
of North Carolina and other Southern States, irre
spective of race or politics."
The matter of national importance in the issue is
the effect such wholesale restriction of suffrage in the
South will have upon the apportionment of Repre
sentatives in Congress after the next census. There
were in 1890 something more than 1,750,000 colored
men of voting age in the United States, and in iÃq6
they were allowed by law to exercise the franchise
everywhere except in Mississippi and South Caro
lina. Since that time Louisiana has adopted a consti
tutional provision which debars most of them in that
State from voting, and now North Carolina seems
about to follow the example.
It is not likely the people of the North will be will
ing to concede to the South representation in Con
gress upon the whole of her population when up
ward of one half of it is excluded from the exercise
of the franchise. The issue will not arise until ths
time comes for apportioning Representatives after the
census of next year, but it will be sure to arise then,
and is being already discussed among far-sighted poli
ticians. â¢

DUMB ANIMALS' FRIENDS.
The Society's Lady Members Pre-
paring a Fine Entertainment.
The ladies interested in the organiza
tion of the bands of mercy of this city
have decided to give an entertainment in
the second week of November. The or
ganizers are all members of the Society
for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals
and their mission is to educate the young
sters to be kind to dumb animals. That
they have been successful Is apparent
from the fact that nearly every pupil in
the public schools is a member of one
of the bands. In order to give a tangible
evidence of membership they concluded
to furnish all who belong to the bands
with a badge. A? these cost money the
entertainment was arranged to ruise
funds, and from appearances it will be a
financial and social success.
At a meeting of the ladies yesterday
reports were made by the several com
mittees to the effct that excellent talent
can be had. Several artists from the
Orpheum will assist in the entertainment
and Rabbi Voorsanger. ex-Congressman
Maguire and others will speak. It is be
lieved that the exercises will be opened
by Mayor Phelan. The ovent is to take
place in Metropolitan Temple.
HE HAS ONE FRIEND.
Mecheli's Fish-Catching Dog Would
Follow Him to Jail.
The up-country Judges have no use for
men who violate the fish and game laws,
for when one comes before them the sen
tence is usually severe. E. Mecheli, the
Italian who was arrested' for killing fish
in Russian River by the use of dynamite,
was taken to T'kiah. where he pleaded
guilty before a Justice of the ppace. As
the Superior Court was in session he was
taken before that tribunal and again ac
knowledging his guilt was sentenced to
pay a fine of $250 or serve 125 days in the
County Jail. This was the follow who
was assisted in his illegal business by a
large black dog. which retrieved the dead
fish from the water for his master.
Deputy Vogelsang had no little difficulty
in slipping the prisoner away from the
dog at Cloverdale',. the faithful animal
seeming determined to follow his owner
even to prison. If Mecheli is unable to
raise the money to pay the fine it is be
lieved that the "officers "will allow the dog
to share Its master's imprisonment.

Beard and Bacilli.
The man with the stunning brown beard

TO REMOVE RUSTâ A. S., City. The
following is given as a recipe for the
removal of rust from nickel-plated war* :
Cover the stains with oil or grease for a
few days: then remove rust by rubbing
with a little ammonia. If this doee not
remove the rust, try very dilute hydro
chloric acid, and when dry polish with
tripoli or whitening.

The tig log raft 'hat has been such a
menace to navigation for a week past
la on its way Into port. It was sighted
a.t 4:30 a. m. yesterday thirty miles north
west of Point Reyes in tow of a steamer,
presumably the Czarina. At the hour
named the steamer North Fork was mak
ing her way through the fog from Eure
ka to San Francisco. There was a good
eight-knot breeze blowing and a heavy
swell from the northwest made things
lively. Captain Bash did not see the raft
until those on the steamer towing it burnt
a flare-up. From the general appearance
of the steamer he took her to be the
Czarina, and so reported as soon as he
docked at Mission-street wharf.
The northwest swell was going down
rapidly when the raft was sighted from
the North Fork and incoming vessels re
port that outside yesterday it was almost
a dead calm. In that event the Czarina
and her heavy tow should reach here this
afternoon if all goes well. It will be a
relief to everybody when once the un
wieidly affair is safe in port. The steamer
Walla" Walla, me ship Oriental and the
bark Terris Bj Thompson all had narrow
escapes from running into it.
The.tug Reliance was coaled last night
and went out to assist the Czarina in tow
ing the raft into port. Captain Seaman
of the collier must have done somÂ« lever
work in picking up the hawser of the
raft. There was about sixty fathoms Oi
chain and then about 100 fathoms of a
mar.ila hawser attached to one end or the
structure. In order to secure this a
grappling line had to bÂ« passed under the ,
raft ana towed along until it caught in ;
the towing line. This operation must
have been successfully pertormed by
Captain Seaman and his crew. - Â»-

Bert Ullom of Company G. Thirty-first
Regiment, was treated at the Harbor Hos
pital twice Wednesday night. On the first
occasion Dr. Robinson sewed up six cuts
In his left wrist and hand and started
him on his way to the camp. Early in
the morning he was found lying at the
door of the hospital with the bandages off
his wounds and his wrist dislocated. Dr.
Robinson took htm in again and fixed
him up. He is expected back it the hos
pital, as he seems unable to keep out of
trouble.

THE BIG LOG RAFT ADRIFT.
CHEF MARKET
INSPECTOR GUIS
IS DISCHARGED
Summarily Removed
by Health Board.
The Board of Health held a special ses
sion behind closed doors last night for.
the purpose of investigating the culpabil
ity or incompetence of Chief Market In
spector William Gans, who testified It the
trial of Jesse Meyers; arrested for killins
Immature calves for sale, that the said
calves, or one of them, was at least a
mouth old. Other witnesses, inci'.ding the
mar. who sold the calves to Meyers, testi
fied at the trial that the calves wVre but
three days old, and on the strength of
their testimony Meyers as convicted.
The action of Gans in the matter had a
suspicious look, inasmuch as he seemed
determined to protect Meyers, who is said
to enjoy h's personal friendship, and vig
orously objected to the action of Dr.
O'Rourke. veterinary to the board, and
Officer John Butterworth, who made the
arrest, declaring that tEBj" wen encroach
ing on his authority. \
After hearing the testimony of
O'Rourke. Butterworth and Assistant
Mark- inspector Barron. the board sent
for Gans to tell his side of the story.
Gans admitted that he had sworn that
the Jersey- calf was a month old. but
sought to save himself by explaining that
he might have been mistaken, as he had
made only a superficial examination of
the condemned veal.
The explanation was not satisfactory",
and for fear that Gar.3 might make other
mistakes of a similar character he was
removed from his position by unanimous
vote. Dr. O'Pourke was directed to per
form the duties of Chief Market Inspec
tor pending the appointment oi a succes
sor to Gans.
The board discussed the necessity of a
new City and County Hospital building
and came to the conclusion that the Su
pervisors should be requested to provide
the money for this purpose, regardless of
the dollar limit or anything else. On mo
tion of Dr. Keeney Dr. Payne was dele
gated to drawMip a communication to the
Board of Supervisors, asking that a cer
tain amount of money be provided for the
construction of new hospital buildings,
and stating that if this were not done
the City and County, Hospital would be
at once condemned and closed.
John A. Hoejr. Inspector of the depen
dent poor at the City and County Alma
house. Â«ent in his resignation, which was
accepted. The place will probably be
tilled a* the next meeting of the board.
C. O. Southard was appointed assistant
chemist to the board, the appointment to
dat" from September S.
Mrs. S. B. Re^d was appointed matron
at the City Receiving Hospital, vice Mrs.
Esmonds, removed.
The salary of Dr. W. F. Rarbat. bac
teriologist to the board, was Increased
from $100 to $150 per month.*

HUNT'S BULLETS FIND
THEiR SECOND VICTIM
E. R. KNIPPEXBERG DIES SUD
DENLY.
Deceased Did Not Know He Had Been
Shot by the Man Who
Killed Elliot.
The bullet wound sustained by Saloon
keeper E. R. Knippenberg in the shooting
which happened in the Young America
saloon, St^uart and Howard streets, Sat
urday afternoon, proved fatal last even
ing. Knlppenberg was shot by Robert L.
Hunt, an insane teamster, at the same
time that George Billot, another occu
pant cf the -king-house, was killed by
a buliet from the same revolver.
The details of the shooting were
brought out at the in<;uest over the body
of Elliot on Tuesday. Hunt, harboring
some imagined grievance against the El
liot brothers, entered the saloon and
tired three shots. At iirst it was sup
posed that only Elliot had been struck,
and it was not until an hour after the
affair had taken place that Knippenberg
discovered that there was a bullet in his
body. He had been removed to his home
and Dr. Peter A. Kearney summoned.
The physician found that the shot had
entered "his right breast just below the
nipple, but, until the hour of the saloon-'
keeper's death, was unable to locate it.
Knippenberg raliied until yesterday,
wht.-n he had a severe sinking spelll.
Win.- his case was considered serious,
none of his family believed that death
would come as soon as it did. Last even
ing about 4 o'clock he became unconscious
an-1 died five minutes afterward.
Hunt has been charged with the mur
der of Elliot, and upon favorable reports
which reached police headquarters h-i was
simply booked with assault with intent to
kill in the case of Knippenberg. This last
booking will be changed to-day, when an
autopsy on the body will be performed.
Knippenberg was about s*l years of age,
leaves a wife, and was well and favorably
known. He conducted a saloon for some
time and belongs to several prominent
German societies.
OCCIDENTAL'S ANNIVERSARY.
A Banquet in Celebration of the Nine
tenth Year of Its Institution.
Court Occidental No. 5576. Ancient
Order of Foresters, celebrated the nine
teenth anniversary of its institution by a
banquet last night, and about 100 'mem
bers and several specially Invited guests
partook of the choice menu that was set
before them. Neil O'Neill, the chief
ranger, was toastmaster, and he called
upon George W. Lunt. high court
treasurer, to respond to the toast "Our
Order." Past Chief Ranger C. J. King to
respond to "Our Country" and Joseph
Moscrop, past chief ranjrer, to respond
to "Our Court." after which, J. Hender
son, The financial secretary, on behalf of
the court, presented to William Wallace,
the court's recording secretary, a fine
. gold watch and chain, appropriately In
scribed, as a token of appreciation of nine
teen years' faithful service in the court.
Mr. Wallace has served the court in Var
ious cities, and in each he proved a
conscientious worker, and he has a re
markable record which is that In all that
time he has rever been absent from any
meeting. During the evening there were
vocal selections by the Occidental Quar
tet and responses to impromptu toasts
by a number of those present.
The celebration ceremonies were ar
ranged by W. J. Bloa, M. Silvia, J. J.
Smith, George W. B. Stirlin. George
Friedman, John H. Horwood and D. S.
Besecker.

SMASHED BOX CARS.
Railroad Wreck at Ocean View in
Which No One Was Injured.
Engine No. 1262. acting as a. helper to
the San Jose mail train which left here
at 2:30 yesterday afternoon, collided with
some empty box cars at Ocean View, sus
taining considerable damage and smash
ins; the front ends of the cars.
The empties were on the side track and
; when the engine attempted to make a
flying switch to the siding the engineer
miscalculated the distance and bumped
into the cars before he could stop. The
' ends of the cars were wrecked and the
forward- part of the engine was badly
smashed. No one was injured.

DESERT THEIR VILLAGE.
Filipinos at the Fair Insist Upon
Taking in the Sights.
A Filipino village without Filipinos is
not a howling success, and just now Su
perintendent Cummlng of the Mechanics'
Fair la having: his hands full trying to
keen the nipa huts at the Pavilion inhab
ited with the little brown men from Lu
zon who belong with them. The Fili
pinos have suddenly become imbued with
American ideas. They want to wear the
clothing of the white man. think that
they are a little out of place living in
their native habitation, and. despite all
the efforts of the fair management, they
Insist upon wandering away from th^ir
Village and taking in the sights. Super
intendent Cumming called a halt last
night, and as a result of the conference
two nt the Filipino hoys will take an in
voluntary trip back to Manila. â¢
Last night a new feature was added to
the fair in the shape of a mandolin- or
chestra of twenty-four pieces that played
during the evening in the Filipino summer
house near the entrance to the pavilion i
The Indians from Mendocii County con
tinue to attract attention. Mora Castle
In coal, the Eifel Tower in tin cans, and ;
the art gallery, with Miss Raymond's cor- !
net solos, still command a great share of !
the public's attention. To-day's pro-'
gramme of music includes the "Battle of j
Gettysburg/ with a regiment of veter
ans from the Philippines, led by one of !

Belvedere Cove To Be
a Marvel of Light.
BRILLIANT WATER CARNIVAL
ARKS ; AND FLOATS UNIQUELY
DECORATED FOR PARADE.
All Manner of Naval Architecture
Will Lend Itself to the Making
of a Modern Venice by
Night.
The cove at Belvedere will be a modern
Venice on Saturday evening-, when the
stage is set for a third renewal of the
Venetian Water Carnival. Nothing has
been left undone by the numerous com
mittees, each charged with special dv- ;
ties. The illuminations, the elaborate
| programme of appropriate music, the dec
orations of the yachts and of the homes
; on the hillsides have all been planned and
will be' ready when the carnival is set
in motion at S:3O p. m. to-morrow.
The cruiser Marblehead and the tor- '
i pedo-boat Farragut will lend picturesque
I ness to the scene on the water. The men
j of the Third United States Artillery band
will contribute appropriate music during |
i the festivities. The splendid voice of t
; Frank W. Thompson, who impersonates t
! the Doge of Venice, will also be hear.d
to advantage. He will be assisted by the j
' following chorus:

MEXICAN CELEBRATION.
The Eighty-Ninth Anniversary of
Republic's Independence to Be
Celebrated To-Night.
The Club Patriotico Hidalgo has con
cluded all arrangements for the grand
celebration which will take place this
evening in Washington Square Hall, cor
ner Stockton and Union streets, in com
memoration of the eighty-ninth anniver
sary of the independence of Mexico.
The literary and musical exercises will
be very interesting. Among those who
will take prominent parts are: Senora
Ellsa A. de Navarrete. Senora Luz D. de
Warde. Professor Lornbardero. his noted
quartet and others. Senor Olmeda will be
floor manager and his assistants are:
Senors Ezqueda and Pereda; Dr. F. Marti
nez, honorary president: Carlos Monels
vais. president: E. M. Navarrete. vice
president, and G. D. Kotta. secretary.

COMMERCIAL AND FINANCIAL NEWS
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver unchanged. Financial quotations as before.
All cereals neglected and more or less weak.
Large quantities of Hay detained over in Oakland.
Beans and Seeds as previously quoted.
Potatoes show a fezu narrow changes.
Vegetables continue in liberal supply.
Batter, Cheese and Eggs stand the same.
Receipts of Wine Grapes rather larger.
New grades and prices for Raisins.
Steady demand for the general run of Dried Fruits.
Hams less active. Bacon and Lard in good demand.
Hides and Tallozv in brisk inquiry and firm.
Meat market unchanged.

val for wheat or barley to Europe, 31s Bd.
The Charles R. Wilson loads lumber at Grays
Harbor for Honolulu; Star of Italy, lumber on
the Sound for Adelaide, 555.

Dried Fruit in New York.
Mail advices from New York say: "Prunes
are attract attention at present. ' but there
Is an Impression that the trade may be de
celved by present reports and that the output
may be "larger than anticipated. Considerable
sales are reported, but it cannot be learned
that any of them are coming to this market.
It is a difficult matter to get quotations on
large Quantities. Growers seem In no hurry
to dispose of the.lr crops, the present outlook
appearing unusually favorable, according to
mail advices from the coast The principal de
mand at present is for the large sizes, and
prices on them are held high. The proportion
of large sizes appears to be higher than last
season.
"Apricots are quiet at the moment, though
prices are. held firmly. The bulk of the crop
is out of first hands and Jobbers do not care
to move holdings.
"Peaches are dull and the market weak, with
Scarcely any one willing to make firm offers for
large lots. The supply has been heavily in
creased by the throwing back of green fruit by
canners. Improvement may come later, but few
have any faith in it now.
"Raisins are firm, but trading is small- be
cause of the uncertainty regarding opening
prices. Confidential advices confirm the figures
already published, but add that a premium
may be chared for first shipments. Another
statement is that nothing can be depended upon
until prices are actually given out.
"New crop figs are expected toward the last ,
of this month, and prices are quoted at lli&14c
to arrive. - -
"Other lines are quiet, but are firmly held
in anticipation of improving business this
month."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has fallen rapidly over the conn
try west of the Sierras.. It has risen over Utah
and Nevada. An area of low pressure overlies
California and Western Arizona. The tem
perature has fallen from six to ten degrees
over the interior of California. In the great
valleys of California conditions are still favor
able for fruit drying. Showers have occurred
in Northern Arizona, and a trace is recorded
at Wlnnemueca. In the northern end of the
Sacramento Valley a moderate norther in blow
ing, and a maximum velocity of 3* miles per
hour from the north Is reported at Red Bluff.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours, ending midnight, September 15. 1599:
For Northern Californiaâ Friday; warm
er in the valleys; fresh northerly winds; wester
ly winds on the coast, with fog.
For Southern Californiaâ Fair Friday; warm
er In the interior, with northerly winds; fresh
westerly winds, with fog on the coast.
For Nevada â Fair; warmer' Friday.
For Utahâ Fair; warmer Friday.
For Arizona â Fair Friday.
Ff.r San Francisco and vicinityâ Fair Friday,
â with fog in the morning: fresh westerly winds.

EASTERN MARKETS.
New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK, Sept. 14.â greater part of
the Rains In prices which were established yes
terday and durras the early part- of to-day
wore wiped out In the latter part of the day's
trading. A Derlod of almost stagnation suc
ceeded until the announcement of the fixing of
the St. Paul dividend at the regular rate. The
large Felling of grangers Induced by this news
Wai met by supporting orders, which rallied
prices from the lowest. Losses are the rule on
the day, however, which extend to a point In
many active stocks. The rate for money was
nnt listed above the lower level established in
yesterday's relapse, and the reaction in prices
could not be attributed to calling of loans. It
vii- rather due to a reconsideration of the
optimistic view engendered yesterday by the
easing In the money rate and the sentimental
effect of the Treasury Department's decision
to anticipate the payment of October interest
on Government bonds. Yesterday's upward
movement has' been reduced to its true propor
tions of a bear scramble .to cover short con
tracts after having oversold the market In the
rush of heavy liquidation on the preceding
days. Estimates varied widely as to whether
the liquidation proceeded to sufficient- lenghts
to re-enforce the banks against the present and
future demand u;.on their cash resources.
Money continuc-s to move to the interior and the
banks have parted with upward or $4,000,000 to
the Sub-Treasury since last Friday. There was
no evidence to-day of a further urgent con
traction of c red Hi but the outlook for the fu
ture is Dint further liquidation may become
necessary. Foreign houses are reported to have
put put considerable sums in loans yesterday
and to-day, nnd this may account for the com
parative firmness of the foreign exchange mar
ket. Discounts hardened to-day In London and
Berlin in spite of the improved condition re
flected by the Hank of England's weekly state
ment, and London bought stocks moderately In
New York in continuation of the fortnightly
settlement. The failure to increase the St.
Paul dividend Is accepted as an Intimation that
the present prosperous condition of the rail
roads are not so well established but that ample
provision must be made against a recession.
The extreme decline from the early high point
ranged from 1 to 2 points very generally among
the active railroads. *
The grangers and trunk lines were especially
affected. In the specialties movements were
Wider. Brooklyn Transit was under pressure
all day and in the final dealings broke its pre
vious low record on the movement and fell
to IM 4. at which point It closed, 4lÂ«. below yes
terday. Tennessee Coal ranged over 3V4 points
and closed with a net lops of 2.
Sugar, Tobacco, the local municipal stocks,
People'B Gas and the Iron and steel stocks were
all wr-ak before the close. A number of oh
scure stocks showed wider movement than the
average. Pullman, Twin City Rapid Transit,
Mobile and Ohio and Pacific Coast common and
second' preferred . gained between 2\i and 4
points, and Knickerbocker Ice dropped 5V4.
The United States milling: stocks continued
their upward course and gained Hi each. Bonds
were only moderately active. Total sales, par
value, $1,985,000. United States bonds were un
changed In bid auotattons.

Chicago Grain Market.
I CHICAGO, Sept. 14.â Uncertainty in regard j
I to the September wheat situation made wheat ,
rather feverish at the opening, and for a short
! time a really large business was done. Cables
were stronger ."and higher, and this and yes
' terday's bulge In September due to the eleva
tor fight gave a tone of bullishness to trading
that was reflected In slight opening advances
in the deferred futures. The bullishness giad
! ually disappeared when Weare, who was so
prominent in yesterday's September deal, was
seen to have considerable September to sell.
This at once relieved the tension in that re
spect, and the September premium, at one time
%c over December, gradually disappeared al
together. Rumors of private settlements of j
September among big shorts were current, and
that gave rise to the belief that the conges- |
tion had been relieved. The inclination among j
local professionals was apparently to sell, and
support came principally from the outside, 1
most of the commission-houses having buying
orders early; The September tension once re- 1
moved, the market became exceedingly dull, j
New York reported good acceptances of last |
: night's cable offers and the Northwest reported
a good cash demand, but the market in a gen
era! way showed heaviness until the primary
receipts, 1,341,000 bushels, when positive weak
ness developed. December opened a shade high
er at 71%(&71%c. It advanced to 71% C, then
sold down to 'â â¢ 14c, closing at 70%@70%c. Sep
tember opened at 72Â®72%c and declined to 70 7 ic,
closing %c lower at 71% c. Weare was a bid
der for September late in the session, which
caused the rally in that option. Large pur
chases of wheat at Southwestern points ' for
shipment to Chicago were reported.
Corn was irregular and fairly active. A fea
ture was the strength of September, growing
! with recent strength' of September wheat and
! the small stocks of corn, which made shorts
' inclined to get out. Prices advanced sharply
early, but heavy liquidation by longs followed,
nearly all' the advance being lost. The mar
' ket was rather easy late in the session, fol
lowing wheat. December closed a shade lower
j and September He higher.
Oats were very dull. December closed un
! changed from yesterday. . â  .-, ." â .-â ."
The market for provisions was neglected.
j There was some firmness early, but the market
sympathized with the late weakness of wheat .
and corn. Most of the light trading was In
the January product. At the close January
pork and ribs were a shade lower and January
lard unchanged. -

Demand for SheeP'kiris.
LONDON, Sept. 14.â Two thousand five hun
dred bales of sheepskins were sold at auction
in Mincing Lane to-day. The serectlon was
poor, but there were several fine parcels
which brought good prices. Practically all
the offerings were sold at prices ranging
above the previous sales. Merinos were half
penny to 3d and a few choice lots nearly Id
higher. Fine erossbreds %Â©*. coarse-^ *?
higher. The home trade was the. best bujer,
France and Germany, taking moderate lines.
The strong demand is attributed to the health
iness of the home market and the activity to
secure wools from the skins was continued.
The scarcity is: increasing and there is dif
ficulty in finding an adequate supply.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Securities were rather quiet on the morning
session and variations were of a fractional
character. In the afternoon Giant Powder de
clined to $75 874.
Mining stocks were quiet and not materially
changed. The telegram from the pump said:
"At 6:30 o'clock this morning the water was 34
feet below the 1950-foot level station. No. 1
elevator has been running continuously and is
regulated to hold the water at about this point,
while elevator No. 2 is being altered and low
ered. No. 2 elevator, rearranged, will be low
ered this afternoon to the lÂ»su station. The
pressure and discharga columns taken out are
on that level."
.The Spring Valley Water Company has de
clared a dividend of 42 cents per share, payable
on the 20th.
The New York and Honduras Rosano Min
ing Company has declared a dividend of 10
cents per share, payable on September 20.
Four mines will pay dividends to-day as fol
lows: Empire State of Idaho. $324, 625; Modoc
of Colorado, $10,000; Republic of Washington,
$31.1)00, and War Eagle of British Columbia,
$26 250.
The recently elected directors of the Security
Savings Hank of this city met and organized
on the 12th as follows: President, Wlnneld b.
. Jones; vice president, William Alvord; secre
! tary. S. L. Abbot Jr.
According to the last monthly reports of the
Alaska-Mexican and Alaska-United mines, the
former crushed 14,666 tons ore, valued at $19,
--679 and turned out 299 tons sulphurets, valued
at $10,201. The ore averaged $2 2n per ton. The
gross earnings were $32.17S and the working ex
penses $19,080. The latter mine crushed 24,134
tons, valued at $35,725. and yielded 645 tons
sulphurets, valued at $20,163. The ore averaged
$2 52 per ton. The gross earnings were 188,806.
One of the mills dependent upon the water pow
er had to hang up some of its stamps five days
during the month because of the short supply.
Over $200,000 was paid out In dividends by
sugar corporations at Honolulu on the Ist, as
follows:

The letter from the Justice mine for the week
ending September 12 states that on the 370-foot
level the sputh drift, started 75 feet from the
face of west crosscut No. 1, was advanced six
feet during the week, making its total length
41 feet. The face Is In porphyry, with streaks
of quartz.
The Confidence, Challenge Consolidated and
Consolidated Imperial mining companies con
tinue to prospect the west country at Gold Hill.
New. through the old surface tunnel and its
connections. East crosscut No. 2. from the
north surface tunnel, Is out 50 feet, 20 feet hav
ing been made during the week. The face
Bhowg clay and porphyry. The west crosscut
from the surface tunnel is now out 2050 feet,
having been advanced 26 feet during the. week.
The face is in porphyry.
In the Belcher mine no work was done during
the past week, owing to the timbers of the gal
lows frame having become so "rotten that they
were obliged to take down the frame and re
place it with a new one. This work is now be
ing done.

Notice to Mariners.
Robinson Point Fog Signal and Post Light,
Washington.
Notice la hereby given that the Bounding
of the fog signal at this station, a twelve-in-h
steam whistle (giving blasts of six seconds
duration, with silent intervals of fifty-four
seconds) located on the easterly extreni-
Maury Island, about nine miles north ol
coma, Washington, will be discontinued ;
pairs to the machinery from October 15,
to November 1, 1899. both dates inclusive.
This notice affects the list of lights and fog
signals. Pacific Coast, 1899. No. 15S, pa?p ?:: :
also the list of beacons and buoys, Pacific
Coast, 189 S. page 70.
By order of the United States Lighthouse
Board. W. L. FIELD.
Commander, U. S. N., Inspector Thirteenth
Lighthouse District.

ARRANGE THE
BOND DETAILS

Been Prepared.

90S Broadway, Sept. 14.
H. W. Watkins. president of the West
Oakland Improvement Club, stated at the
meeting" of the Associated. Cluba that it
was ais opinion that the details of the
bond issue are already arranged and that
ill the work being done by the eight im
proved clubs in the association, is use
less.
The '.â â .-- to-lay that Mr.
Watkins Is mistaken. "The details of the
bond issue." said Mr. Snow, "will depend
to- a great degree on what the improve
cnent "clubs and every other progressive
Â»iement in the city recommend. I can say
without fear of contradiction that not a
single detail has yet been decided or even

" - r wV"are always credited in Oakland
wt*h "being slow, but the issue of bonds
is certainly one at those things that must
r.ot and shall not if I can heip it, be hur
r<ed through without mature deliberation.
[ do not know at present of any one thing

the city is agreed, except perhaps the con
struction of an Intercepting sewer along
Che estuary. No one knows what parkd
we want, what wharves we need, or what

at this time. Nearly everybody has a

framatur* conclusion and vote to put her
-eif into- debt for forty years without
knowing very clearly Just what she is
-
"it" is a grave mistake for any one tn
imagine that any little gang of politicians
or capitalists or any other element is
going to get together and lay down tne
law as to now bonds shall be issued. This
is pre-eminently a matter for the whole
people and 30 io-ng as I am Mayor the
whole peo^Me shall pass upon it and shall
help with their advice the decision of

this vÂ«Â»ry important bond question."
At the next meeting of the Associated
Clubs the executive committee, which 13
composed of one delegate from each club.

3CS Broadway. Sept. 14.
Wliliain T. cfagehora, one of the pio
neer merchants of Oakland, called at the

Jefferson Park is located between Sixth
UXd Seventh. Jefferson and Grove streets.
'Tn that neighborhood," said Mr. Sage

In the Mayor's office there is now on

and east triangles of Jefferson Square can
be converted into a girls' and boys' play

vided for all the ordinary fittings of play
grounds and the Oakland Club composed
of ladies interested in child culture are
heartily in favor of the public playground.
The club has published its reasons why
It favors the using of a portion of the
park for recreation grounds. In their re
port t3 the following paragraph:
In Oakland we have a ciimats eminently

tounha at the year. It is urged that there la
za necessity for playgrounds for children; that
they a!rÂ«ail7 have too much play; that there

ars facts, but net all the facts. The streets
are no's- the common playground; really the
3nly playground for Uttiis children during- the
period oÂ£ strongest character growth. The
{nrrrvtg* at the street !a corrupting. Pla7 ta
be of greatest service should be wisely directed.
Children should be surrounded by <;bjeÂ«:ta that
wtll cultivate the beat and purest ideaia.

HE DODGED "MISTS"
AND THE "UNKNOWN"
Oakland Office San Francisco Call, Ml Broadway, Sept. 14.
ALFRED WILKIE. the well-known tenor, is one of the few men who has
had the pleasure of reading his own obituary. It cornea to him all the
way from Chicago, where many years ago WllkJe was tie organizer of
-he Saturday Night Club. A faw weeks ago Wilkie wa3 reported as be
ing m his deathbed, and half a dozen physicians had declared that he
would not see the sunrise an the following morning. When this bulle
tin was issued, some energetic newspaper men. knowing that Wilkie had a
Chicago musical record, put on the wire the item that the papular tenor
passed into the unknown singing a. aong.
On the strength of this dispatch, a musical critic of the windy city wrote
Wilkie 1 - obituary. It was sent to Mr. Wllkis to-day., and, after reciting
th* facts of his final sickness, It says:
"His soul was full of music and fellowship. OnÂ« at â ': i last survivors of
that old Saturday Night Club is not surprised to hear that the last act of
Alfred Wiikie was a song as the mi3ta gathered about him and he passed
into the unknown."
Mr Wilkie was discharged a few weeks age from a. sanatorium, i- . earr
ing the loss of about twenty pounds of fl^eh, he is a long way from the
"mists" and the "unknown."

tions cf a mysterious burglar, who a few

articles stolen include a necklace of gold.
an old gold watch valued chiefly as a
keepsake, a Masonic charm, a fob chain,
a diamond iocket and numerous other
trinkets, aggregating a total value of
abont 1300 or $400. A peculiar feature of
the aflair ia the fact that the jewelry was
not all taken at the same time, the dia
mond locket beln? first missed. The next
day the gold watch was missing and later
the other articles. An investigation baa
failed to reveal any evidence of the burg
lar having- forced an entrance to the
house, from which fact the detectives are
led to believe that the smooth worker
may be some ore familiar with the prem
ises and interior of the residence.

KAARSBERG ELECTED
BASEBALL CAPTAIN
BERKELEY, Sept. 14.â "Pete" Kaars
berg has been chosen captain of the Uni
versity of California baseball team. Th*
selection was made by the members of
the team last night, and their choice in
favor of Kaarsberg waj unanimous. ThÂ«
new captain's reputation on the diamond
ts quite well known, tie having played
pitcher for the last two seasons on the
university nine. His election give 3 much
satisfaction to the Berkeley men. Be
sides playing fullback in last year's foot
ball eleven he also holds the office of
treasurer of the Associated Students.

OAKLAND, Sept. 14.â For the Brst time
in twenty years K. â â " Snow is not an
officer of the First Congregational
Church. For some time past the Mayor
has been the president of Its â  board of
trustees, but at his ursent and persistent
request he wa3 left off the board elected
at the annual meeting- last night.
"I have seen the church grow until it
has become the present powerful organi
zation." said he. "and have taken a hand
In directing- Its finances until the valuable
property Is entirely free of incumbrance,
the current expenses are all provided for
and tn addition there is a comfortable
surplus In the church treasury. My mu
nicipal duties now take all my time, and
I have been forced to withdraw from the
executive department of the church."
The new board of trustees consists of
C. S. nCarmen, F. M. Smith. Georare EC.
Collins, R. H. Chamberlain, J. C. Agard,
S. T. Alexander and J. B. Richardson.

SMUGGLING IMMIGRANTS.
Mexican Laborers Imported to Work
on Railroads in Arizona and
California.
Samuel I. Peerry of the United States
Immigration Board at Victoria passed
through this city yesterday on his way
to the Mexican frontier to investigate the
alleged KmiiKfding of Mexican railroad la
borers across the line Into the United
States at points of railroÂ«d construction
between Tncson, Arizona, and Bakers
fteld. Cal. A short time ago Deputy
Immigration Commissioner A. de la
Torre of this city visited the border near
San Diego and found that the smuggling
of Mexican labor into California was go
ing on at a lively rate. He reported the
fact to the Commissioner General at
Washington, and Mr. Perry was detailed
to look into the matter.
COMMISSIONER GWIN RETIRES.
Has Been Asked for His Resignation
to Take Effect on the First.
As foreshadowed In The Call several
months ago, news came from Washing
ton yesterday that Shipping Commissionn
( r "William M. Gwin will be request
ed to hand In his resignation to take
effect on the Ist of October. Mr. Gwin
was appointed by President Cleveland six
years ago, and was afterward placed in
act as moderator. Rev. Dr. Mackenzie will
deliver the sermon. Rev. Mr. Carson will
charge the pastor and Rev. Dr. Hemphlll
will charge the people.
Rev. Mr. Thomas is quite young, but he
has already occupied several pastorates.
He is a graduate of Lawrence University,
Wisconsin, and Lane Seminary, Cincin
nati. He was first a pastor at Jackson,
Ohio, and then occupied the pulpit in the
Fifth Presbyterian Church in Cincinnati,
where he remained three years. Then he
was pastor of the Bethlehem Presbyterian
Church in Minneapolis. He comes to San
Francisco from Minneapolis.

THEATER WAR TAX.
Special Agent Thomas Will Procure
the Rearrest of Lessee McDonald
of the Alhambra.
Internal Revenue Agent Bert M.
Thomas is not satisfied with the decision
of United States Commissioner Heacock
in the theater war tax case. In which
Henry McDonald of the Alhambra Thea
ter was the defendant. McDonald was
lessee of the theater and was arrested
for refusing to pay the war tax of $100
per year.
His attorney contended that the pro
prietor of the theater building should pay
the tax and not the lessee. judge
Heacock decided tbat McDonald"s at
torney was right.
Mr. Thomas says that If the derision
stands it will lose A large sum of money
to the United States, and that all over
the country the lessees and not the pro
prietor is made to pay the tax. Mr.
Thomas proposes to file an information
against McDonald and throw the contro
versy Into the United States courts for
decision.
The men of Chicago spend $3,500,000 a
year for shaves.

THREATENED WITH ARREST.
; .Deputy Fish Commissioner Locks
Horns With His Late Prisoner.
Deputy Game and Fish Commissioner
L. N. Keroheval is having trouble with
a man whom ho arrested a week ago for
violating the laws by having a set wire
net in the San Joaquin River. On the
7th inst. he found J. D. White, Frank
Winter and C. L. Toyer at the net. which
he was watching, and arrested them on
the spot. The three were taken to Tracy
where, acting upon the advice of the
Justice of the Peace, he carried them on
to Stockton for trial. Toyer was dis
- missed by the Stockton magistral
he immediately demanded that the assist
ant District Attorney issue a warrant for
the arrest of the CommiPsion. rs' officer
for false and illegal imprisonment. This
the assistant refused to do.
Kercheval states that his acts were
proper, being advised to do as he did by
the Justice at Tracy, who did not want
! to handle the case a." the jail at that place
| nad been burned down. In regard to the
j imprisonment of Toyer, he says that
I under tne circumstances that it "was the
only thing he could do. In fact, he sub
mitted the facta to the District Attorney
and the latter stated that there was
ample evidence to hold him. Mr. Kerche
\al came back to town yesterday to tes
tify In rr.other case in which the com
mission is Interested, hut he Pays that
If he is wanted at Stockton ho will wili
ingly meet the charges that may be made
and he :s sure that his course will be
Justified.
In a new electric bell of German origin
the automatic make and break is a sepa
rate apparatus which may be placed in
the main battery circuit, so that one will
suffice for all the bells worked by each
battery.
NEWS OF CAMP, FIELD AND TRANSPORT
TROOPS WILL
PARADE ALONG
THE BAY SNORE
Line Will Form at
Eleven O'Clock.
The great question for discussion at the
Presidio yesterday was the big- parade.
It. has been decided to form the troops
at the foot of the old parade plain, their
right resting on the bay and the line ex
tending up toward the volunteers. This
will bring them facing west. The various
commands have been ordered to be ready
to form at 11 o'clock, and there will be no
delay.
The order requires "undress" uniform,
and this phrase has set commanding offi
| cers talking. Just what undress uniform
Is Is a little uncertain just at present.
It may mean leggins and campaign hats,
or it may mean no leggins and fatigue
| caps; or it may even allow 'the dress the
I volunteer regiments have been wearing
1 very much of lateâ campaign hats, leg
gins, blue trousers, no blousts and a dark
blue shirt, set off sometimes with a red
' necktie. â¢
The Twenty-sixth Infantry will be the
! next to arrive at the Presidio. It was
recruited in the far East and rendezvoused
at Plattsburg barracKs, New York. It is
commanded by Colonel Edward Rice.
The regiment has been some days on the
way out here, but if nothing happens it
should reach here to-day or early to
morrow. Whether or not it will go into
camp here is a question, for unless the
seven remaining companies of the Thirty
fourth get away before it arrives, the
; Twenty-*lxth, or part of It, will have to
<go into temporary quarters in the de-
I tention camp on Angel Island. No troops
I have been sent there yet, for the camp
; was established for any volunteer regi
ment returning, from the Philippines thai
might be put under quarantine
The Belgian King, the transport to
which the remaining companies of the
Thirty-fourth have been assigned, is
booked to sail to-day. If she can get
away the Thirty-fourth will be out Just
in time to make room. There will be
another regiment ' In, the Thirty-third
from San Antonio, Texas, about Septem
ber Â£0, and the Thirty-third will <b e in
danger of banishment to Angel Island if
â  there is any delay in the leaving of the
Thirty-first, booked to sail on the Grant
;on September 18. No westbound regi
ment has so far arrived from the East
i on time, however, so the coast will very
probably be clear when the Thirty-third
I arrives.
Company F of the California regiment
1 bad ' i of Minnesota to lunch yesterday.

MISS LIZZIE PRUE IS
RAPIDLY RECOVERING
SHE HAS BEEN REMOVED TO DR.
LANE'S HOSPITAL.
Yellow Journalism Is Seeking to
Make Capital Out of the Un
fortunate Girl's Attempt
on Her Life.
Miss Lizzie Prue. the young lowa girl
who attempted to commit suicide In the
Grand Hotel Tuesday night and again
"Wednesday morning, is now well, on the
road to recovery. Wednesday night she
had several attacks of heart failure, but
Dr. Robinson never relaxed his" care for
an instant and yesterday morning he was
able to say she was out of danger. Dur
ing the day her brother telegraphed to the
manager of the Grand Hotel to send her
home at once, but the doctor recom
mended a rent of a few days before send
ing her on the long journey.
Miss Denny, who became acquainted
with Mips Prue in the Grand Hotel re
mained with her friend all night and all
of yesterday. She offered to take the un
fortunate girl to Benicia, but the yellow
Journal came on the scene and for ad
vertising purposes had her removed to
Lane's Hospital.
There is really nothing more to be done
for Miss Pnle, as all she wants is a good
rest. To Dr. Robinson belongs all the
credit of bringing her back to life. From
11 a. m. Wednesday until 6 a. m. Thurs
day he was in constant attendance upon
Miss Prue and had it not been for his
skill she would undoubtedly have died.
The would-be Filicide was in a much
healthier frame of mind yesterday. She
now wants to live and is sorry she tried
to kill herself. Miss Denny is 'still of the
opinion that she was out of her mind
when she took the poison and Miss Prue
says she remembers nothing but an over
whelming desire to die. After spending a
few days in the hospital she is going back
to her home in Waverly, lowa.

United States Treasurer Ellis H. Rob
erts has sent an interesting bit of in
formation to Assistant Treasurer Julius
Jacobs of this city relative to making
payment on certain United States bonds.
lit- Bays in substance: "You may antici
pate the payment of coupon 4's, due on
the Ist of October, beginning with 18th
inst. Interest checks on the same loan
will go forward by mail on the 21st for
payment by you when presents. Give
immediate attention and advise public."

enjoyable one. The ladies of the two
companies were all present, and they saw
that no soldier was neglected. The offi
cers of both companies spoke, and several
of the men lent their aid in an impromptu
programme.

passed by courts-martial:

United States army, found guilty of for
gery, sentence: "To be dishonorably dis
| charged the service of the United States,
forfeiting all pay and allowances due him
i and to be confined at hard labor at such
place as the reviewing authority may
! direct for three months."
Private William C. Miller, Battery N,
Sixth United States Artillery, found
i guilty of absence without leave and lar
ceny, sentenced: "To be dishonorably

States, forfeiting $10 pay and to be con

thority may designate for one year."

Sixth United States Artillery, found guilty

over him; of escaping from the guard

one month and to forfeit three months'
pay."
The Yosemlte Club will receive those of
its members belonging to the returned
First California Volunteers to-morrow
evening in the clubrooms, 105 Ninth street.
The members of the club await the event
with great interest, for besides being able
to welcome the fighting boys they have
prepared a first-class programme. Each
member of the club is entitled to invite
a friend to be the guest of the club for
that evening. Refreshments will be
served.
Next Tuesday evening, September 19, at
Metropolitan Hall, an address will be de
livered by Rev. William D. McKinnon on
"The Californians in the Philippines."
There will be a short entertainment pre
ceding the same, in which the sextet of
the First California Volunteers and prob
ably also the regimental band and other
members of the company will participate.
The address is for the benefit of the Cath
olic Truth Society, which spent many
thousands of dollars in caring for the sol
diers before they left for Uie Philippines.
TEACHERS SEEK TO HAVE
SCHOOL FUNDS INCREASED
ENCOURAGED BY SUPERVISOR
HOLLAND'S ATTITUDE.
Secretary Simpson of the Board of
Education Issues a Call for a
Meeting.
Secretary J. A. Simpson of the Board of
Education yesterday sent the following
notification to principals and teachers of
the public schools:
You are hereby notified that the Board of
Kducation has authorized that the pupils be
dismissed and the schools closed Friday after
| noon, September 15, lMÂ»a, and that a meeting
â  of the principals and teachers of this depart
ment be held in the auditorium of the MiHslon
High School, corner of Eighteenth and Dolorea
streets, at 1:80 o'clock p. in., Friday, Septem
ber 15, 1899. Question of school funds. Attend
ance Is requested.
The object of the meeting is to urge
upon the Supervisors the necessity of in
creasing the appropriation of the School
Department. It is understood that the
pedagogues are desirous of having $6000 a
month added to the appropriation as al
ready fixed. They are very much encour
aged at Supervisor Holland's attitude, in
the matter. His intention to submit
amendments increasing certain appropria
tions, as published exclusively in yester
day's Call, includes $70,(M> additional for
the public schools, and this is practically
the amount sought for by the teachers.
Supervisor Aigeltinger, when seen yes
terday, stated that he will stand by the
figures as presented by his colleague, and
his support may he counted upon. A me
morial will be addressed to the Supervis
ors expressing the wish of the teachers
on the subject before next Monday's
meeting, when the lax levy must be posi
tively fixed.
Orphanag-e Reception.
The managers of the Maria Kip Orphan
age will receive their friends next Sat
urday afternoon, September 16, between
the hours of 2 ami 5, at the orphanage,
520 Lake street, corner of Seventh ave
nue. The children have arranged a short
programme to amuse their visitors and
will also nerve light refreshments. All
persons interested in this most worthy
charity for girls are invited to be present.
The South Dakota band has most gener
ously donated its services for the enter
tainment. Miss Shipley will give some
very fine selections on the cornet.

HER CLEARANCE
Allowed to Sail From
Hongkong.
Special Dispatch to ThÂ« Call.
iÂ»AoxjimunjiX| nepi. i*. â Clearance pa
pers have been allowed the Tartar â¢ at
Hongkong. It is expected that she
will proceed to the United States at once.
The Information that clearance had been
allowed came in a dispatch to Adjutant-
General Corbln this morning from Colonel
Metcalf, commanding the. Twentieth Kan
sas, and the troops aboard the trans
port.
It is supposed clearance papers were al
lowed upon the suggestion of the British
Foreign Office to the British Governor at
Hongkong that it would be unwise to In
terfere with American transports. An
other dispatch from Colonel Metcalf in
reply to one sent yesterday states that
the Tartar was no more overcrowded and
that the food was as good as on other
transports leaving Manila. He said that
the trouble arose among the discharged
regular soldiers who were returning home
on board the ship.

ing dispatch has been received at the

MANILA. Sept. 14.â Secretary of the

Davidson, commanding the Paragua, re
ports sharp engagement at Balemao. Ves
sel struck many times by rifle shots; no
casualties. Paragua silenced insurgents'
lire in twenty minutes; range from 400 to
900 yards. Occasion, capture Filipino

_ , WATSON.

CITY TEMPLES
WERE CROWDED
TO THE DOORS
Day of Atonement
Celebrated.
PRAYERS FOR THE DEPARTED
â¢ IMPRESSIVE SERVICES MARKED
[ THE OCCASION.
'â¦
â¢ Choirs Were Augmented and the
f Sound of the Shofar and the
Chants of Cantors Aided
( Worshipers.
$
5 The . ceremonies marking- Tom Kippur,
j the Jewish day of atonement, closed at
', I sundown last night. The rigid fast that
â  | was enjoined Wednesday night continued
> ' until the close of the services yesterday.
! | The services in the synagogues lasted con
( I tinuously from 10 a, m. to sunset.- In
t i every synagogue worshiping Jews
' thronged to" show their loyalty to their
' ancient faith. Choirs were augmented
> and assisted largely to make the services
! impressive. So large was the demand for
â¢ room that it was necessary to reserve
| seats for seatholders in several syna-
g ogues. Memorial services were held in
> every synagogue and special prayers were
; offered at the request of those who have
, lost relatives by death during the past
| year.
At Tempel Emanu-El Rabbi Yoorsanger
; urged the Jews to be loyal to their relig
ion. "I tell you it is high time," said
Rabbi Voorsanger, "that you should un
derstand that the religion of Israel is not
merely a religion of an idol before which
to kneel once a year. It is not the relig
ion of gratitude to express when God has
been good to you. It is the religion of
the virtuous man, of the virtuous woman,
who understand that all life, from its be
ginning to its ending, should be the ex
pression of God in man. It is the religion
of conduct. It is next a question of loy
alty, and next of self-respect."
Rabbi Voorsanger denned loyalty as "a
virtue that does much to improve the re
lations between man and man and the
agencies for the interpretations of God's
law." "The higher virtue of our lives
is loyalty to the parent who â¢ gave us
birth. God pity the man who has not loy
alty for his mother, for his flag and for
his religion. As the mother suggests
protection and love, so does the flag sug
gest protection from worldly danger*; and
religion- suggests the undying hope of the
soul. The sacrifice of loyalty is, at the
same time, the sacrifice of self-resp-.ct.
I do not mean, by loyalty to religion, sim
ply attending the synagogue. 1 mean a
loyalty of heart and of affections; a loy
alty that will come from the sentiment
within us that we will stand by our cause,
no matter what happens, and never let
it go under."
The speaker also urged self-respect. A
man who did not respect his religion does
not respect himself. He had much to
learn who had too little self-respect. A
people who had not self-respect would be I
judged harshly by the world. The cause
was lost that was not founded upon per
sonal conduct, loyalty and communal and
personal self-respect. Cantor Stark and '
Organist Sabin conducted the musical ex
ercises. An orchestra took part .in the
memorial services.
At Sherith-Israel Synagogue Rev. Dr.
Nieto compared the Jews of two periods,,
the Jew of ancient history and the Jew
of the present day. When the barriers
were rent and the Jew of olden times was
liberated the world ceased to molest him
in the practice and observance of his
religion. His hand was taken by his
brother man and he was recognized.
When the ghetto fell what was to be
come of the Jew? He had a difficult task,
for he knew only his own language; he
had to make his way; yet all the facili
ties for success were dented to him. How
could the Jew progress?
He has been brave; he has been true
to religion. Dr. Nieto wished to deny
that the Jew had been either cowardly or
Irreligious. The charge of cowardice upon
the Jew of history was based upon a mis
taken conception of hifmility. The charge
that the Jew would not fight for his
country had been proved to be false. In
Russia he had joined a service that had
given him no honors and has left his
family degraded. Germany was served
by the Jew, but no honors were given him
in return. In France a Jew was not ac
corded his rights. No wonder that he did
not care to serve in the army or navy
of France. But in England and America
the Jew, being accorded his rights, had
proved to be as brave as a Hon. In San
Francisco there were at the time of
speaking 300 Jewish soldiers who were
wearing the uniform of the United States.
and of Samar are praying for the speedy
arrival of the Americans and will wel
come the hoisting of. the American flag.
It is said that as a result of the forcible
collection of taxes by the emissaries of
the insurgents, who take all they have
got, the natives are in a state of semi
dtarvation. They have no faith in the
Tagalos and they earnestly desire Ameri
can protection.
An insurgent leader, General Luzban,
has bolted to Japan, taking with him
$2firto collected by him for the insurgents.
The agents of the insurgents endeavor to
force the natives to join their forces,
which they will not do. The condition in
the island, it is asserted, is rapidly ap
proaching riot and anarchy, as the heavy
and continued drag upon them in the
form of tribute exasperates them, and
they, the letter says, threaten desperate
disturbance If it continues.
TROOPS TO COME HERE
EN ROUTE TO MANILA
LOS ANGELES, Sept. 14.â Santa Fe
officials here have been informed that
six special trains bearing the members of
the Thirty-second Volunteer Infantry
Regiment will leave Leavenworth to-mor
row for San Francisco. The trains are
due at San Francisco on Wednesday, the
20th, and will come by way of Mojave.
A train of eighteen cars will leave San
Antonio, Texas, loaded with men en
listed there for the regulars, and will
pass through here over the Southern Pa
cific route on Monday for San Francisco.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 14.â Secretary
Root has ordered the Twenty-eighth Vol
unteers, Lieutenant Colonel Leonard,
from Camp Meade. and the Twenty-ninth,
Colonel Hardin, from Fort McPherson,
for San Francisco, to embark for the Phil
ippines. The order is the same in form
as that issued to other regiments.
Ship May Be Saved.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 14.â A cable dis
patch received here from Quartermaster
Miller at Manila gives information con
cerning the cable ship Hooker, .which was
wrecked on a reef near Corregidor Isl
and more than a month ago. It says:
"Position of Hooker on the reef shows
slight alteration for the better. Bids for
saving the vessel and cargo opened Sep
tember 15. Have sent to .Hongkong for
appliances."

Cantor Davis conducted the music of the
service with an augmented choir.
Rabbi Levy preached at the Geary
street Temple morning and afternoon.
During the afternoon service he asked
his hearers to consider whether they had
passed through the day of atonement in
the proper spirit. Fasting and prayer
were unavailing unless the heart hart
been purified. Had the whole day found
his congregation in a contrite spirit:
Had they taken the attitude of the dying
man who said: "I have sinned, I have
transgressed, I have gone astray . He
urged upon all hearers that they should
harbor the proper spirit that would make
the season of real benefit to their fellow
men and to themselves. At the forenoon
service Rabbi Levy made allusions to the
dead and to the duty of living. Rev J.
Rabinowitz was the cantor. The choir
consisted of eight.
Rabbi Isador Myers of Congregation
Ohabai Shalome. took for his two topics
for the day "Paradise Lost" and "Para
dise Regained," discussing the first-named
at the morning service and the latter in
the afternoon. The Biblical story of the
Kin in the Garden of Eden and the ex
pulsion of Adam and Eve from the
garden was reviewed. God asked the
same Question in modern times that he
a skrd of Adam: "Where art thou?
Awakened conscience led to atonement.
Discussing the Day of Atonement the
rabbi said that it was a means to bring
man back to a state of happiness. It en
abled him to make his peace with God.
The children of Israel returned like doves
to their religion. To be like the dove
they must be humble and gentle. Man
can best find comfort in religion. D. Mey
erson was the cantor. The choir con
tained eight volunteer singers.
Services were held at B'nai B nth Hall
by Rabbi Abraham of the Septorlm con
gregation, composed of orthodox Jews
from the Orient. The services at NeVah
Zedek 943 Mission street, were conducted
by Rev. Mr. Rosenbaum. Jewish soldiers
were in attendance at all the synagogues.

The gasoline launch which had brought
him moved a,way, and not wishing to take
the responsibility of throwing him, over
hoard to the sharks, the officials' were
obliged to allow him to remain.
General Reeve, in explanation of his
act, said that he had not been warned
not to go on board the Sheridan, and that
he was not aware that he was violating
any law or regulation. Tho Minnesota
boys were so glad to see him, he said,
that they pulled him over the rail and
forced him to remain on board all night
against his will.
1 "nited States Attorney Coombs, to
whom the matter was referred, was
making an investigation.
A Parish Reception.
A reception will be tendered to those
members of the First California Volun
teer Regiment and the Heavy Artillery
who reside within the parish of St.
Joseph's Church on Wednesday evening
September 20, at the hall, corner of Tenth
and Howard streets. A committee of ar
rangements consisting of R. P. Doolan
Joseph T. Dowling and M. Bohanon
representing the St. linoent de Paul
Society, is preparing a programme. Rev
Father McKlnnon, chaplain of the First
California Regiment, will be among the
speakers. Admission will be by compli
mentary tickets, which may be obtained
from any of tiie members of the St. Vin
cent de Paul Society.
GRAVE CHARGES OF
CORRUPTION IN MANILA
Former Clerk TTndÂ«r Auditor of Pub
lic Accounts Says the Govern
ment Is Being Robbed.
DENVER, Colo., Sept. 14.â Napoleon E.
Guyot, late private of Company G, First
Colorado Volunteers, who during the last
three months of 1898 served as clerk un
der Major Kilbourn. and later under
Lieutenant Colonel Potter, auditor of pub
lic accounts. Manila, publishes this even
ing a signed statement in which the
gravest charges of corruption are made
against the American officials in Manila.
He says an examination of the vouchers
forwarded to Washington will show that
exorbitant prices are paid for all kinds
of supplies purchased in Manila- that
vast quantities of high-class wines and
other supplies have been purchased os
tensibly for the Spanish hospitals, while
in the American hospitals only the coars
est supplies are furnished. He says
courts-martial of private soldiers charged
with selling Government property have
been stopped because they would result
In the exposure of official "rascality. The
robberies, he asserts, will aggregate ah
enormous sum.
